4.19        The preceding table shows total project costs by component.
Project cost estimates are based on: ti) up-to-date cost estimates by
supervising consultants for on-going civil works initiated under Loan 2829-CO
and those started with FVN's own funds; (ii) preliminary engineering designs
for the works not yet contracted; (iii) published suppliers' current equipment
and spare parts prices; and (iv) prevailing fees for individual technical
assistance consultants (US$10,000 per man-month for expatriate consultants and
US$5,000 for local). Physical contingencies were calculated at 10 of the
contract price for all civil works. These contingencies were estimated at
US$30.1 million. Price contingency was estimated at 5Z p.a. (in US$
equivalent) for the period 1992-96 with an estimated total of US$41.5 million.
E.    Economic Evaluation
(a)   Roads
4.20        All roads included in the rehabilitation, paving and construction
program have been subject to an economic evaluation. For roads already in
execution under Loan 2829-CO, and which will be completed under the proposed
project, an economic evaluation was carried out during the appraisal of that
loan and all showed rates of return exceeding 12t. Nevertheless, all these
roads were subjected to an economic reevaluation using the vehicle operating
costs subroutine of the Highway Design and Maintenance Model developed by the
Bank. This reevaluation takes account of all the changes since the original
evaluation in terms of project cost, vehicle traffic and road condition and
was based on comparing vehicle operating costs before and after the project.
The economic operating costs were calculated for each type of vehicle, for
each type of road condition (bad, fair, good), geometry (mountain, hilly,
plain), and surface type (paved and unpaved) (Table 19). The evaluation shows
that for paving projects started under Loan 2829-CO, the rates of return vary
from 7% to over 501 with a weighted average of 232 (Table 15). Two of the
projects show a return of less than 10%. For these projects, a second
analysis was conducted taking the investment already made as bygone. The
returns on the remaining investment turn out to be 12% and 31Z, respectively.
For rehabilitation projects started under Loan 2829-CO the returns vary from
17% to over 50% with a weighted average of 31%. The weighted average economic
return for all projects started under 2829-CO and with FVN's funds (base cost
US$147.9 million or 67% of the road component), is 26%.
4.21        All road sections which belong to the north-south corridor along
the Magdalena River connecting Bogot& with the Atlantic ports of Cartagena,
Santa Martha and Barranquilla were analyzed as one project, since completion
of the corridor as a whole is required to realize the benefits from through
traffic. In this analysis, in addition to normal growth based on traffic in
the period 1980-89, generated traffic was estimated at 3% annually. The
benefits from the paving program of 120 km of the 511-km corridor are derived
from reduction in operating cost for both long-distance traffic to the ports
which is now using an alternative route, and local traffic, which is now using
the existing unpaved roads. The rate of return on the project is 32X.



- 28 -
4.22        The above returns, while very satisfactory, are below those
projected during appraisal of Loan 2829-CO, which were 20Z to 1001 for
rehabilitation works and 20Z to 70Z for paving. The reduced returns are due
mainly to the inadequate cost estimates under Loan 2829-CO, which were caused
partly by incomplete and unsatisfactory engineering designs. This will not be
repeated under the proposed loan given the advanced stage of projects under
execution and the setting of standard engineering norms and technical
specifications, as well as standards for road geometric designs, which will
result in a revised engineering manual and consistent road design standards.
The submission every six months of updated investment plans will prevent the
start of additional projects without adequate resources, which in the past has
led to delays in execution and cost increases.
4.23        For projects which have not yet started, a detailed analysis has
been conducted using the HDM model. Traffic for each sub-project was
projected based on historical trends and expected generated traffic. Using
engineering data and climatic conditions, as well as traffic characteristics
and cost parameters, the model simulated the operating conditions for each
sub-project and calculated vehicle operating costs. For rehabilitation
projects, the benefits of the proposed investments (including overlays at a
cost of US$9.4 per square meter whenever road roughness reaches a certain
level) were measured against the alternative of carrying out only routine
maintenance (at a cost of about US$500 per km) and road patching (at a cost of
about US$7.6 per square meter). For paving projects, the benefits of the
project were derived by comparing the scenario under the proposed project with
a base case which consists of routine maintenance of unpaved roads (grading),
spot regravelling, and gravel resurfacing every 3-4 years (at US$5 per square
meter). For the six new construction projects the economic analysis takes
into account the reduction in vehicle operating costs because of the improved
road conditions and shorter distances as well as time savings because of
reduced congestion. The average economic rate of return on all projects which
are yet to be contacted is 401 (Table 15).

- 29 -
(b)   BridRes
4.25        The base cost of the bridge construction and rehabilitation
program amounts to US$9.7 million. All new construction projects which exceed
US$500,000 (seven projects totalling US$7.1 million) have been subject to a
detailed economic evaluation. Of the seven projects, the two largest were
analyzed as part of the total investment in constructing the north-south
export-import corridor (para 4.21); the economic return on that project as a
whole is 32Z. For the other bridges, the benefits from new construction are
associated with savings in both vehicle operating costs and time. It should
be noted that only time savings related to work were taken into account. The
bridges proposed for new construction replace existing bridges which are in
danger of collapse. In case of collapse, traffic would have to be diverted to
an alternative route. Three of the five bridges are on heavily trafficked
roads with ADTs averaging over 1000 vehicles per day and the other two on
medium-traffic routes of 400 vehicles per day. The substantial cost involved
in diverting traffic to alternative routes yields rates of return exceeding
SOZ for all these bridges.
4.26        Two bridges which were proposed by MOPT were deferred for further
analysis: Zambrano Bridge on the northern section of the Magdalena River
(estimated cost US$10 million, length 1500 m) and the Puerto Arturo Bridge on
the Guaviare River in the South (estimated cost US$5 million, length 600 m).
While their tentative returns were in the range of 16-20%, additional
refinement of the traffic assignment is required in both cases. For the
Arturo Bridge, transport by ferry has to be looked at again as an alternative
to the bridge construction.
(c)   River Training
4.27        The river improvement component will have three major effects:
(a) it will allow present producers and shippers to use the river year-around
instead of nine months and avoid the higher cost of alternative transport by
road; (b) in the case of petroleum products, the project will save the cost
involved in mixing low-value heavy oil with higher-value light condensates in
order to be able to ship the heavy oil by pipeline; and (c) it will reduce
operating costs for existing traffic by allowing larger convoys with more
heavily loaded barges, higher speeds and quicker turnaround of equipment. The
traffic projections are based on detailed interviews with producers and
shippers. For petroleum traffic, projections do not include the new refinery
which could begin operations by 1997. However, the expected increase in coal
exports which will use the Dique Canal has been factored into the analysis.
4.28        If river transport is handled efficiently, its cost advantage over
road is substantial. The economic cost of transporting a ton of general cargo
for the 650 km run from Barrancabermeja to Cartagena is about US$6.40 by river
(UScent 1.0 per ton-km) but it is about five times higher, (5 US cents per
ton-km), by medium-size trucks on hilly roads in average condition. The
economic analysis prepared by MOPT assumes: (a) a reduction in operating
costs for existing traffic (US$1.92 per ton for liquid cargo, US$2.24 for
fertilizers, US$3.87 for malt and US$0.56 for steel); and (b) savings
resulting from avoiding the need to add light products to heavy crude



- 30 -
condensates in order to ship the latter by pipeline. About three barrels of
light crude need to be added to ten barrels of heavy crude in order to enable
shipment by pipeline. The resulting economic rate of return on the river
component (including the cost of river ports improvements and navigation
safety aids), which has a total base cost of US$26.6 million, is 202.
V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A.    Proiect Execution
(a)   General
5.1         The proposed loan will be made to the National Highway Fund (FVN).
FVN is an autonomous legal entity, able to contract financial obligation. PVN
has, as its legal representative, the Minister of MOPT and shares MOPT's staff
and structure. Throughout this report, reference made to FVN as the entity in
charge of project execution should be deemed to be made to FVN in its legal
capacity to execute the policies and programs of MOPT, including the project.
The project will be carried out in about five years from the second half of
1991 through the end of 1996. Responsibility for the preparation and
execution of specific subcomponents will be delegated by MOPT to its various
directorates as follows: General Highways Directorate (DDC) for the roads and
bridges civil works, the planning and execution of the road maintenance
program and the supervision of technica.l studies and assistance; Directorate
of Navigation and Ports (DNYP) for the river training and river port
improvement component; Subdirectorate of Equipment for purchase and
rehabilitation of equipment; Offico of Planning for supervision of economic
studies and related technical assistance; and Directorate of Industrial
Relations for the training program. Overall project coordination and follow-
up will be done by a newly formed Project Implementation Unit. Environmental
control will be provided by a new Envirorment Unit (EU) within MOPT.
(b)   Proiect Monitoring
5.2 The Governmernt has decided to establish a special mechanism in order to
ensure satisfactory project execution and avoid recurrences of over-
programming, project Alays and cost increases.  Accordingly, the Fiscal
Council (CONFIS) within the Ministry of Finance, acting on behalf of the
Government, together with the National Planning Department (DNP), will enter
into a contract for a Performance Plan (Plan de Desempefio) with MOPT. The
contract will include: (a) a review of each road project which falls below
802 of target in terms of physical progress; (b) a review of each project
whose costs exceed by more than 20X the initial cost estimate; (c) monitoring
of the relationship between annual revenues of FVN and total commitment level;
(d) review of implementation of the highway maintenance plan; and (e) follow-
up on progress in achieving the institutional improvement tasks under the



- 31 -
action plan which forms part of the Loan Agreement. The Ministry of Finance
will employ special consultants to provide the day-to-day project follow-up
and, together with the National Planning Department, will carry out project
reviews twice a year. As stated in para. 3.8, agreement was reached during
negotiations on the draft contract to be signed between MOPT, DNP and the
Ministry of Finance. This document will be signed prior to signing of the
loan documents. Agreement was also reached during negotiations on annual
Droiect reviews with the participation of CONFIS, DNP, MOPT and the Bank,
which will cover the topics of road network decentralization, organizational
restructuring of MOPT, project physical progress, institutional strengthening
program, environmental mitigatory actions, progress on the program on road
user charges, the investment and the maintenance programs and their
corresponding financing. In addition, during negotiations, an action plan was
agreed with the Borrower on specific institutional and technical aspects.
Furthermore, agreement was reached during negotiations that the Bank will have
the right not to approve new sub-projects if it considers the overall
execution of the project unsatisfactory. The table in the following page
summarizes the main topics to be monitored during project implementation.
(c)   Project Implementation Unit
5.3         Overall project coordination, supervision and administration under
the on-going loan (2829-CO) has not been satisfactory. Project information
has not been collected in time from the field, and the administrative section
of the External Credit Office, which supervises processing of loan
disbursements, has not been in a position to examine the technical aspects of
project implementation. To resolve this issue, a new Project Implementation
Unit (PIU) will be created within the External Credit Office with a permanent
staff of five to monitor project progress, resolve outstanding issues,
coordinate among executing departments and agencies and maintain continuous
up-to-date financial information. As a condition of loan effectiveness, the
PIU will be fully staffed and in operation.
(d)   Civil Works
(i)   Roads
5.4         Most of the road civil works to be carried out by contract under
the project are already under way and involve about 2,000 km of roads. These
works will be supervised by engineering consultants under existing contracts
and subject to the general oversight of the DDC. For the twelve road projects
approved by the Bank and not yet contracted, final engineering studies will be
required by December 31, 1992. This forms part of the action plan agreed
during negotiations.

(ii)  Bridfes
5.5         For bridge works, final engineering is available for all minor
projects. However, the seven major projects, which all exceed US$500,000,
hove only preliminary engineering. The preparation of final engineering for
this component will be done by the Subdirectorate of Projects within the DDC.
Final engineering should be completed by the end of 1992. Most of these works
will be carried out by contract. Supervision of all bridge works will be
under the Subdirectorate of Supervision within DDC.
(iii) River Training
5.6         The General Directorate of Navigation and Ports (DNYP) within MOPT
will be responsible for the preparation of bid documents based on engineering
studies already completed. Private contractors will carry out the river
training and dredging works under the supervision of the DNYP. Maintenance
works, after the project component is complete, are expected to be transferred
from the DNYP to private contractors (para. 2.43).
(e)   Road Maintenance
5.7         While the project does not finance road maintenance, the equipment
component was designed in accordance with the 1991-95 road maintenance plan
prepared by MOPT. This plan forms an integral part of the project. The
Maintenance Planning Unit within DDC will continue to be responsible for
updating the plan and will be assisted by consultants under the technical
assistance program. The execution of the plan will be in the hands of the
districts. The Subdirectorate of Equipment will be responsible for ensuring
that the equipment acquisition and rehabilitation program proceeds as
scheduled.
5.8         As stated in para. 2.18, MOPT has undertaken, in the context of
the Performance Plan, to take all necessary steps to modify its structure so
as to incorporate all maintenance functions into one new Directorate of
Maintenance. This change will ensure coordination among the planning,
monitoring, and execution functions of road maintenance, including maintenance
of the road equipment. The new scheme will also incorporate the Project
Implementation Unit (para. 5.3) and the Environmental Unit (para. 5.25) into
MOPT's organizational structure. This restructuring of MOPT will be necessary
even if decentralization is carried out.
(f)   Equipment
5.9         Acquisition of equipment and spare parts will be controlled by the
Subdirectorate of Equipment, which is responsible for the vehicle fleet.
Rehabilitation of all equipment will be done by MOPT's own workshops, which
are adequate. Acquisition of laboratory equipment will be carried out by the
central laboratory within the DDC and equipment for the axle weight control
program will be under the supervision of the Programming Office of DDC, which
will monitor the installation and training, while operation will be left to
the districts.



- 34 -
(g)   Road Safety
5.10        The road safety component was designed by the Programming Office
of DDC. The materials which will be purchased under the project according to
the agreed road safety plan, will be transferred to the districts, which will
install the vertical and horizontal signalling using their own staff.
B.    Procurement
5.11        The civil works components include contracts for about US$369
million, of which some US$188 million have already been contracted either
under Loan 2829-CO or with FVN's own funds following Bank procurement
guidelines. On-going civil works which were contracted by MOPT using its own
funds but were not awarded according to Bank guidelines, did not qualify to be
included under the loan. Civil works contracts for new road rehabilitation
and paving are estimated to cost between US$0.6 and US$13.7 million. Bridge
repair and construction contracts vary from US$0.1 million to US$1.5 million
and the river training and river ports improvement contracts range from US$2.3
million to US$12.8 million. Contracts for all civil works exceeding US$4.5
million will be procured through International Competitive Bidding (ICB), in
accordance with Bank procurement guidelines, with the rest, up to an aggregate
of US$191.7 million, under Local Cor?etitive Bidding (LCB). The appraisal
mission reviewed LCB procedures and found them acceptable. In particular, LCB
procedures allow bidding by foreign contractors. The threshold for ICB was
determined based on the experience with the most recent Bank loan, where
foreign contractors showed little interest in contracts below US$5 million.
5.12        The estimated cost of individual road and bridge sub-projects
approved during appraisal is shown in Tables 15 and 16. In line with para. 4
of Appendix 1 of the Bank's procurement guidelines, MOPT will inform the Bank
before agreeing to a change in any contract which will raise its price by more
than 201 over the original cost (in June 1991 prices) for contracts already in
execution, or over 201 of contract price for new works. Agreement on the 201
benchmark was reached during negotiations. Approval of new sub-projects by
the Bank for financing under the undefined component will be subject to
submission of detailed engineering, economic and environmental evaluation, as
well as the Five-Year Investment Plan showing that the FVN has the funds
necessary to carry out the construction of the new sub-project according to
schedule (para. 3.7).
5.13        Prequalification of civil works contractors will be done for all
contracts under ICB and for contracts under LCB exceeding US$2 million. As
stated in para. 2.11, a p.equalification document, acceptable to the Bank, was
agreed during negotiations. The prequalification criteria to be used will
include rigorous screening of the contractors' experience and their financial
and technical capability in order to complete the works on schedule.
5.14        Equipment and materials will be procured under ICB, except
contracts not exceeding US$200,000, and totalling up to US$9.5 million, which
will be awarded under local competitive bidding procedures. The appraisal
mission reviewed the LCB procedures and found them acceptable. Items costing
up to US$50,000, and aggregating no more than US$1.2 million, could be



- 35 -
purchased using shopping on the basis of comparison of price quotations fr'Am
at least three suppliers. Local suppliers under contracts awarded under ICB
procedures, will be given a 15% preference in accordance with Bank guidelines.
5.15        All contracts for civil works exceeding US$500,000, all contracts
for equipment under ICB, and the first two contracts for equipment under LCB
will be subject to prior review by the Bank. Thus, prior review will involve
over 90% of civil works (excluding on-going and already approved civil works
contracts) and about 75Z of all equipment. Since the rest of the equipment is
basically spare parts and road safety materials, this percentage is considered
satisfactory. In order to facilitate procurement review, standard bidding
documents for goods and works were agreed with MOPT during negotiations.
5.16        Consultant services for technical assistance and studies will be
contracted in accordance with Bank guidelines. Small consulting contracts
valued below US$20,000 equivalent will be subject only to ex-post review by
the Bank. These arrangements were confirmed at negotiations. A standard
letter of invitation for consultants, standard terms of reference for
engineering studies and supervision of civil works, and a standard form of
consultants' contract, for both foreign and local consultants, were also
agreed at negotiations.

C.    Disbursements
5.18        Disbursement will be made against the following categories of
eligible expenditures: (a) 602 of total expenditures for civil works
contracts; (b) 1002 of foreign expenditures and 90% of local expenditures for
equipment, spare parts, road safety materials and training aide; (c) 1002 of
total expenditures for technical assistance and studies; and (d) 100Z of
foreign expenditures and 65Z of local expenditures for trasning. The
allocation of loan proceeds among the various loan categories is shown in
Table 20.
5.19        Retroactive financing, up to a total of US$18.6 million and US$0.1
million, respectively, are recommended for payments made after September 1,
1991, but before the date of loan signature, for ongoing road civil works
contracts and for consultants eligible for financing under the loan.  This
payment assures continuity in project implementation and avoids work
interruptions due to lack of financial resources and resulting delays and cost
increases.
5.20        A Special Account will be established in Banco de la Repfiblica
with an initial deposit of US$15.0 million (this figure represents
approximately four months of Bank-financed expenditures). MOPT would be
entitled to make payments from the Special Account for the Bank's financed
portion of all expenditures. Applications for replenishment of the Special
Account will be submitted using Statements of Expenditures (SOEs) for
expenditures under contracts for: (a) civil works whose value is the
equivalent of US$300,000 or less; (b) expenditures under contracts for goods
and materials whose value is the equivalent of US$50,000 or less; and (c)
expenditures on consultant services, studies and training under contracts
whose value is the equivalent of US$20,000 or less. Back-up documentation
will be reviewed by supervision missions in the field on a sample basis.
5.21        The Constitutional Assembly has abolished, as of July 1991, the
earmarking of fuel taxes to finance FVN's revenues. This introduces an
element of uncertainty with regard to timely availability of counterpart funds
for the project. To reduce this risk, the Ministry of Finance has undertaken,
in the context of the Performance Plan, to expedite all budget transfer to
complement disbursements under the loan.
5.22        The estimated disbursement schedule for the proposed loan, based
on the assumption that the loan becomes effective in July 1992, is shown in
Table 21.  The estlm-.; profile extends disbursements through mid-1997, or
approximately five years. By comparison, the Second National Road Sector
Project (Loan 2829-CO, US$180.3 million) will take about 4.5 years to
disburse.
D.    Audits
5.23        All records relating to project expenditures will be kept by the
Project Implementation Unit within the Office of External Credits (para. 5.3).
In previous Bank loai!, audit reports for the project were done by the



- 37 -
Contraloria General de la Repfiblica de Colombia within its overall audit of
the Ministry. However, recent audits have been delayed. Therefore, for 1992
and beyond, MOPT agreed during negotiations to contract private external
auditors, acceptable to the Bank, to audit project expenditures, the Special
Account and Statements of Expenditures in accordance with appropriate
accounting principles. The audit will be submitted no later than five months
after the end of each fiscal year.
E.   14onitorinR
5.24        MOPT will submit by April 1 and October 1 of each year an update
of FVN's five-year investment plan and its revenues and expenditures (para.
2.23).  MOPT will also update by September 30 of each year its Five-Year Road
Maintenance Plan (para 2.19).  In addition, the consultants supervising the
execution of civil works will produce quarterly reports on the progress of
each contract. Finally, the PIU will submit to the Bank a quarterly project
progress report showing: (a) an updated listing of eligible road projects;
(b) progress of all civil works contracts and other project components
including latest cost estimates; (c) the status of loan disbursements against
forecast; (d) the amount of counterpart funds allocated to the project in the
fiscal year against the amount actually spent; and (e) the actual road
maintenance budget for the preceding year, the budget allocated for the
current year and physical progress of the maintenance plan for the year. The
Government will monitor project progress separately under a special contract
it will sign with MOPT, and the Bank will conduct annual project reviews with
the participation of CONFIS, DNP and MOPT (para. 5.2).
F.    Environmental Impact
5.25        MOPT has very limited capability to analyze the environmental
impact of road and river projects. It has an Environmental Committee with
five staff reviewing environmental issues in particular projects on an ad-hoc
basis, but it has no staff permanently assigned to the task. It also does not
have up-to-date environmental guidelines to be used by contractors. In the
past, HOPT has turned to outside consultants for a study whenever a major
issue arose. While outside help is a viable strategy to deal with major
studies, MOPT's capability needs to be strengthened. Therefore, as a
condition of loan effectiveness, MOPT will establish an Environmental Unit
(EU) within the Technical Secretariat with qualified full-time staff
acceptable to the Bank. The unit should be provided with sufficient resources
to carry out its task. The EU will be responsible for issuing an
Environmental Declaration for all projects prior to signing of each civil
works contract, declaring whether there are any issues, and what mitigatory
measures are required to address it. The EU will also prepare Environmental
Guidelines to be used by contractors (target date: July 31, 1992) and
supervise the work of consultants when a full Impact Assessment Study is
required. Terms of reference for the EU were agreed with MOPT during
negotiations.
5.26        On the national level, the government agency in charge of
environmental protection is INDERENA. This agency is required by law to clear



- 38 -
each project before it is contracted. In practice, the law has not been
followed. The Bank will require such Approval before approving any new sub-
project. The protection of the rights of the indigenous Indian population is
the responsibility of the Department of Indigenous Affairs within the Ministry
of Government. Coordination between MOPT and these two organizations has been
lax. 'ihe training component of the project provides for attendance at
seminars and courses on environmental issues as well as fellowships for both
MOPT #.nd INDERENA staff.
5.27        The roads, bridges and river works proposed for the new project
have been screened based on their likely environmental impact. As it turns
out, only one major project (the river training works) requires an in-depth
impact assessment study, becauee of the possible effect on fisheries following
the closure of some river branches. Such a study is clearly beyond MOPT's
capability and consultants will be contracted to conduct this study based on
terms of reference acceptable to the Bank. No disbursements will be allowed
for this component until the study is completed and the environmental
mitigatory measures, if needed, are agreed. Another project which raised
potential environmental effects was the highway leading from Rio Baudo to
Nuqui on the Pacific Coast, because of deforestation concerns and the
potential effects on the indigenous population which is abundant in that zone.
This road has not been included in the project pending economic and
environmental studies. With most of the other sub-projects involving
rehabilitation and paving of existing roads and bridges, the environmental
screening indicates that only five other projects require a limited evaluation
and an environmental declaration (dredging of the Canal del Dique, lirador
bypass road, Opogodo Bridge, Magdalena Corridor, and Granada-San Juan de Arana
road), while the remaining projects do not require any analysis.
G.    Proiect Risks
5.28        The proposed project is the tenth highway loan to Colombia and is
based on the experience gained under previous loans. Three main areas pose
potential obstacles to achieving the project's objectives: unsatisfactory
project engineering, lack of a comprehensive system of road maintenance, and
inadequate planning and programming.  Continuance of existing inconsistency in
engineering designs would lead to repetition of cost overruns and delays in
project execution.  Lack of systematized road maintenance would lead to a
cycle of road deterioration and increasing need for large investments. Also,
the present state of programming and budgeting does not ensure use of the same
data by all departments and compatibility between planned investments and
budgetary constraints. The recent elimination of earmarked fuel taxes
introduces an element of uncertainty regarding timely availability of
counterpart funds.
5.29        The proposed project aims at minimizing these risks by either
building on assistance provided under the previous loans or by modifying
existing procedures and work methods. To improve project preparation, the
project includes the introduction of engineering guidelines, based on
documents which are being prepared for road technical specifications, road
geometric design and pavement structure. To improve planning, the rolling
fivye-year investment plan will be updated twice a year, and no new project



-39 -
will be approved unless it is demonstrated that it can be accommodated within
the budgetary constraints. Any cost increase over 20Z will trigger a review
by the Bank and the Government is introducing an innovative supervision
mechanism to follow up on project progress. Also, a new management
information system and a unique data bank will be introduced by consultants
based on work carried out under Loan 2829-CO. To improve road maintenance
practices, a five-year maintenance plan has been prepared for the first time
and consultants will implement the new system, which in prior projects has not
passed the design stage. To minimize the risk of delays in budget transfers,
the Performance Plan includes a commitment by the Ministry of Finance to
expedite budgetary procedures (including a specific time limit for response to
MOPT's budgetary requests).
5.30        As noted earlier, inadequate capacity of contractors has been a
problem in several civil works contracts. To overcome this, new and stricter
standards will be included in a prequalification document which was agreed
during negotiations. Also, the newly created Project Implementation Unit will
improve project follow-up and monitoring, and new stricter guidelines will be
prepared for the consultants supervising civil works.
5.31        A factor which remains a cause for concern is the security
situation. This has resulted in interruption of works on a number of
occasions. Some contingency has been provided in planning project execution
to account for this factor. However, a major further deterioration in
security could severely affect project implementation and the ability to
supervise and monitor works in the field. Nevertheless, recent developments
on the political front give room for optimism in this area.
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
A.   Agreements Reached
6.1         Agreements were reached during negotiations that:
(a)   MOPT will sign by December 31, 1992 the contract with consultants
for the study on decentralization of the national road network and
formulate an action plan, satisfactory to the Bank, based on the
recommendations of the study (paras. 2.13 and 4.15);
(b)   MOPT will recruit consultants to implement the road maintenance
system not later than December 31, 1992 and the system should be
in full operation by December 31, 1993 (para. 2.16);
(c)   MOPT will implement the 1991-95 Road Maintenance Plan and will
allocate the required amounts for road maintenance (para. 2.19);
(d)   MOPT will submit by September 30 of each year an update of the
Five-Year Road Maintenance Plan and by March 31 of each year will
report the amount actually spent for the preceding year and that
allocated for the current fiscal year (para. 2.19);



- 40 -
(-) MIOPT will update and submit to the Bank by March 31 ani September
30 of each year an update of its Five-Year Investment Plan and
FVN's five-year revenue and expenditure program (paras. 2.23 and
4.6);
(f)   MOPT will inform the Bank, and give it the opportunity to comment,
on all new investments exceeding US$10 million which were not
included in the 1991-95 investment plan agreed during negotiations
(paras. 2.23 and 4.6)t
(g)   the Government will adjust, from time to time, retail prices of
gasoline and diesel so that their average price throughout the
year be at least equal to the average border price level (CIF
Bogota for gasoline and FOB Covenas for diesel) (para. 2.28);
(h)   the Government will put into effect and maintain user charges at a
level sufficient to cover 62% of the costs of the public road
network (excluding new construction) during 1992; 78Z during 1993;
and 100Z during 1994 (para. 2.29);
(i)   the Government will complete, by March 31, 1993, an analysis of
road user charges and costs by vehicle type (para. 2.29);
(j)   MOPT will carry out the maintenance program for the Magdalena
River as agreed with the Bank (para. 2.43);
(k)   MOPT will put into effect a revised system of river user charges,
which will cover at least the cost of maintenance of river
transport infrastructure, by January 1, 1994 (para. 2.44);
(1)   the Government will supervise the project implemeintation in
accordance with the Performance Plan (para. 3.8);
(m)  MOPT will carry out the road and bridge works according to the

to terms of reference agreed with the Bank (para. 4.14);

(p)   the Bank will approve new sub-projects only if:  (i) MOPT submits

and 5.12);

(n)   complete the study on performance of road materials and pavement
by June 30, 1994 (para. 4.15);
(o)   submit engineering designs for the remaining new roads included in
the eligible list agreed during negotiations, by December 31, 1992
(para. 5.4); and
(p)   prepare the environmental guidelines for contractors by July 31,
1992 (para 5.25).
B.    Recommendation
6.5         The proposed project constitutes a suitable basis for a Bank loan
of US$266 million equivalent on the terms and conditions set out in Chapters
I-VI of this report.

- 68 -
ANNEX I
Page 1 of 6
COLOMBIA
THIRD NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
Highway Administration
A.   Subsector Orzanization
1.   MOPT is in charge of the construction, improvement and maintenance of
the national highway network. Other major entities are also involved in the
administration of the road network, reflecting the distributic_ of
responsibilities among national, departmental and municipal levels. Twenty-
three Departments are in charge of their corresponding secondary and rural
roads through their Secretariats of Public Works (SPWs). FNCV is in charge of
specific programs for the construction, upgrading, and maintenance of rural
roads in cooperation with departmental and other authorities, through its 24
Regional Directorates. Other ministries and institutions also involved in
rural roads, such as the Ministry of Agriculture, the Colombia Institute for
Agrarian Reform (INCORA) and the Coffee Producers' Association (FIDECAFE),
operate in close coordination with FNCV. The lack of clear definition of
functions between them caused the long neglect of maintenance on secondary and
rural roads. One of the weakest links are the Departmental SPWs, which show
wide disparities in their organizational, financial and technical capabilities
for effectively maintaining the roads under their responsibilities. Under the
Rural Transport Sector Project (Loan 2668-CO) key information was collected
about the length, characteristics, condition and type and volume traffic on
the roads outside the nat'onal system under MOPT. Collecting and processing
of these data continued during preparation of the Second Rural Roads Sector
Project (Loan 3157-CO) and will continue during its implementation.
Furthermore, MOPT completed an inventory including physical condition of the
national departmental and rural network in July, 1990. These data will allow
to reclassify the roads and evaluate the maintenance needs of roads in
selected departments and propose a plan of action for organizing the delivery
of maintenance services, as well as appropriate funding mechanisms.
B.   M3PT's Organization
2.   As shown in Chart I, the Minister of Public Works has the responsibility
for the planning, execution and maintenance of public works through the
corresponding units within MOPT. MOPT's central administration is divided
into two main branches, the General Secretariat, which comprises mainly three
Directorates (Bidding and Contracting, Commercial and Financial and Industrial
Relations) and the Technical Secretariat, which comprises four Directorates
(Highways, Navigation and Ports, Water Supply and Sewerage, and National Real
Properties). The structural separation into two independent Secretariats
produced internal frictions and lack of coordination in the past, which were
eased through the institutional strengthening efforts carried out under the
First and Second National Highways Sector Projects (Annex 4).



- 69 -
ANNEX 1
Page 2 of 6
3.   The Highways Directorate (Chart II) is in charge of MOPT's highway
programs. It comprises four subdirectorates (Projects, Supervision, Equipment
and Rate Assessment) and the 26 regional Districts which carry out road
maintenance by force account and are responsible for supervision of all
maintenance contracts. The operational efficiency of the Highway Directorate
has been strengthened substantially under the First and Second National
Highways Sector Projects and would be further upgraded under the proposed
project. However, an issue that remains unresolved is the road maintenance
structure. At present, the responsibility for road maintenance is divided
among three different organizational units: (i) the Districts, who are in
charge of road maintenance works; (ii) the Maintenance Management Unit (Grupo
de Apoyo de Mantenimiento - GAM) reporting directly to the General Director of
Highways; and (iii) the Equipment Sub-directorate (see Chart II) but with
inadequate coordination between them. Given the close linkage between
equipment availability and road maintenance capability and the need to
integrate the planning and execution functions of road maintenance, an
organizational restructuring is needed.  A new Road Maintenance Directorate
should be created and it should include the Districts, the GAM, and the
Equipment Sub-directorate to enhance maintenance planning and control, which
are objectives of the new Project.
C.   Hizhwav Construction Issues
4.   MOPT's personnel reached a peak of 3,071 employees and 12,200 workers in
1984 and was reduced to 2,639 employees (42% in the districts) and 12,563
workers (91% in the districts) by September 30, 1986. The figures in January
1990 were 4,466 employees (52.5% in the districts) and 8,101 workers (83.5% in
the districts). Of the employees, 795 are professionals (including engineers)
and 829 are technicians. Most of the professional personnel, as well as the
senior staff, are experienced and capable.
5.   Execution of civil works has shifted since the early 1970s from new
construction to rehabilitation of paved roads and paving of gravel roads.
Investments in ne-a construction changed from 85% of the total in 1973 to 22%
in 1990 and an estimated 15% in 1991 and would further decrease under the
proposed Project to about 12% in 1992-95. These civil works are executed
according to outdated design standards. This proposed project includes
technical assistance to establish and implement new design standards (Annex
7).
6.   Detailed engineering and construction supervision are usually carried
out by local consalting firms.  However, detailed engineering for
rehabilitation works has also been done by MOPT based upon road condition
inventories compiled by the Districts. The Districts assign staff engineers
to supervise contracts for desig~a, construction and supervision, under the
district engineer who has been delegated authority to sign payment
certificates on behalf of MOPT. The Sub-Directorate of Projects in Bogota
supervises technical quality of designs, and the Sub-Directorate for
Supervision controls all activities of the Districts through monthly visits by
engineers and administrators who are assigned geographical areas.



- 70 -
ANNEX 1
Page 3 of 6
7.   Under previous Bank projects, quality of works has consistently been
affected by: (a) deficient quality of engineering studies; (b) bidding and
contracting procedures; (c) poor coordination between works and budget
programming; (d) inadequate performance of some contractors; and (e)
unsatisfactory supervision. These factors are analyzed in the following
paragraphs.
8.   Quality of engineering studies is very variable.  In general, the
designs carried out directly by MOPT are acceptable and homogenous. But most
of the engineering studies are carried out by consultants, supervised by
engineers from the Sub-Directorate of Projects as delegates of the Technical
Secretariat. The criteria for supervising of the works are not uniform.
Although the consultants are contracted under terms of reference updated and
improved under the 2829-CO Project, there are no guidelines about the items to
be included in the study nor their scope and content. The so-called "Final
Evaluation Report" is a confusing mixture of technical, legal and economic
recommendations. Furthermore, technical guidelines for design and
specifications for construction are outdated in some cases and are not
specific enough in others. All of these factors have negative consequences in
terms of quality and homogeneity of the engineering studies. To improve these
studies in the future, the proposed Project will finance technical assistance
to implement adequate guidelines and strengthen MOPT's capacity for
supervision of engineering studies (Annex 7).
9.   Cumbersome procurement procedures and delays in contracting, which
affected execution of works during implementation of the 2121-CO Project and
the first years of the 2829-CO Project, were substantially improved by MOPT in
the last two years by simplifying procurement procedures, streamlining legal
services and by preparing standard documents agreed with the Bank; still,
there is room for improvement. The quality of the package of bidding
documents should be improved and the time-lag between the date of the studies
and the corresponding execution of the works could be shortened. To ensure
acceptable quality of civil works, prequalification of contractors is
essential to the bidding process and should be based on criteria which can be
defined with more precision in terms of minimum working capital, volume and
scope of similar works. These and other criteria would be specified by MOPT
in a revised prequalification documents and all contractors (foreign and
local) interested in being prequalified would be evaluated on the basis of
"meet or not meet" evaluation criteria; that is, only those complying with the
minimum criteria would be prequalified. The prequalification documents will
be agreed during negotiations and put in effect as of January 1, 1992.
10. Execution of sub-projects under past loans has been affected by
irregular and often insufficient availability of counterpart funding caused
by the lack of coordination in programming the works. As a consequence, the
large number of legally valid contracts continues to be a major problem. This
problem improved substantially in the last years of the 2829-CO Project,
because funds in the budget were allocated so as to cover the full needs of
some contracts, although it implied a smaller number of contracts being



- 71 -
ANNEX 1
Page 4 of 6
properly funded. Forty-four on-going contracts financed by the Loan 2829-CO
will need financing from the proposed project to be completed. The main cause
of this situation is of a legal nature. The law permits to the Government
sign a contract without specific allocation of funds, by only specifying the
financing resources and the term to complete the works. Furthermore, the law
allows the Government "unilateral modifications of the contract", which must
be accepted by the contractor, who receives an economic compensation. As a
consequence, the Government signs a large number of contracts allocating small
amounts to each one. After awarding and signing the contracts, the Government
pays the advances to the contractor. In most of the cases, the contractor
applies almost immediately for modifications based on some insufficiency in
the bidding documents (mainly in the technical area). The modifications are
frequently confirmed by the supervisor. The terms of the modifications are
agreed but, because of lack of additional funds to be allocated to the
contract, the target date for completion of the contract is delayed and some
work units are eliminated. Lack of some work units, such as drainage, will
cause further deterioration of the works to be carried out and would increase
the chances for landslides and other negative effects. When these occur, the
cost of the contract rises dramatically. This practice may politically be
attractive but it is not sound from a budgetary, economic and technical point
of view. To avoid it in the future, the proposed Project includes an agreed
Highway Investment Plan for the period 1991-95 with all the road works to be
carried out by MOPT in this period financed by IBRD, IDB and other lenders.
Close supervision will be necessary during project implementation to ensure
that appropriate funds are allocated by MOPT to each contract for the entire
period and to guarantee that all the works started will be completed on time
and at a reasonable cost.
11. Implementation of several sub-projects under the two recent highway
loans was affected by inadequate performance of some executing contractors
caused by inadequate financial capacity or equipment. These problems were
often aggravated by delays in payments of invoiced works and price
adjustments. In the two last years, MOPT took corrective measures by speeding
up payments, tying payment of price escalation to satisfactory execution of
the work program and strict application of contractual penalties for
inexcusable delays in works execution. In addition, MOPT is revising the
existing regulations of its register of civil contractors because of
inadequacies found in the evaluation of the financial capacity of contractors.
All these measures, together with the new prequalification documents, as
stated in para. 9 above, and the increase in the number of contracts awarded
by ICB procedures, will contribute to avoid selecting unqualified contractors
as happened in past loans.
12. Supervision of civil works has not been satisfactory in past projects.
It has been mainly focused on determining the monthly payments due to the
contractors and to compare these payments with those established in the
investment programs. In other words, to monitor progress on the budget.
Other aspects shown in the monthly reports by the "interventores" (consulting
supervisors) are: legal and economic aspects; measurements and cost of the
works carried out during the month; evaluation of tasks needed to actually



- 72 -
ANNEX I
Page 5 of 6
complete the contracted works; information on weather conditions during work
execution; and the monthly cosc for supervision. However, poor attention is
paid to quality control. The "Manual para Interventores" (Supervisor's
Handbook) is a guideline with little devoted to quality control. The proposed
project includes the preparation of a new supervision manual. Furthermore,
the cost of supervision under the last three highways loans has been very
high, varying from 9% to 25%, well above the average for other countries. The
main reasons for these high costs are: (i) inadequacy of engineering studies
which require additional studies by the supervision consultants; (ii) MOPT's
practice in the early 1980s to assign individual supervision contracts for
each civil works contract regardless of its size or complexity; and (iii)
delays in execution of civil works, resulting in modest amounts of works
executed each month without reduction in the input for supervision. The
policy adopted by MOPT to employ one consultant for the supervision of several
smaller civil works contracts and the measures being applied to streamline
financial administration of civil works, have reduced supervision costs,
although they continue to be high. The expected improvement on the quality of
engineering studies and contracting procedures under the proposed project,
mentioned in paras. 9 to 11 above, and the implementation of new guidelines
for supervision of works (Annex 7), will further build on the actions started
to correct the deficiencies in supervision of works.
D.   Road Maintenance
13. Until the mid-1970s, the Government gave highway maintenance lower
priority in view of the concentrated efforts to complete the national highway
network. However, faced with the deteriorating condition of the network, a
more positive attitude emerged during preparation of the Seventh Highway
Project (1471-CO). Annual maintenance expenditures increased by about 50Z in
real terms, and maintenance expenditures increased by an average of 5%
annually, in real terms, through 1983. By that year, maintenance expenditures
amounted to 30% of current and capital expenditures on highways. From 1983 to
1986, the maintenance budget remained stable in real terms. In 1987 and 1988
the maintenance budget increased substantially (see following table), but in
1989 it decreased as a consequence of the transfer of about 500 km in urban
areas to the municipalities, and decreased again in 1990 due to MOPT's budget
constraints. (However, for evaluation purposes, it should be noted that costs
for salaries and local materials did not change in local currency at the same
rate than the dollar exchange rate did). The budget for 1991 (base plus
additional) amounts to US$72.4 million, slightly below the needs estimated by
the Maintenance Management Unit (Annex 2J, but above the rate of maintenance
expenditures per km in the LAC Region (mainly because the construction of some
drainage, walls and overlays is included as periodic maintenance).

14.         Under the Seventh Highway Project (Ln. 1471-CO), a major effort
was made by MOPT with the assistance of consultants, to switch from an
administrative to a managerial approach in road maintenance. This effort was
continued under the First National Highways Sector Project (Ln. 2121-CO),
which financed a Pavement Management System and an Equipment Management System
in three pilot districts. The efforts were not successful; the maintenance
management system was not established and the pavement management and
equipment management systems were not extended. Nevertheless, the assistance
left handbooks and guidelines, as well as trained personnel in the systematic
collection and evaluation of information needed to upgrade the maintenance
planning and administration, and compute cost of maintenance operations.
These data have allowed to establish during the preparation of the proposed
Project a system to determine the maintenance programs for 1992-95 (Annex 2)
based on existing needs. The system will be completed and implemented under
the proposed Project (Annex 7).
15.         The Equipment Management System, started under the 2121-CO
Project, will be also completed and implemented under the proposed Project
(Annex 7). Equipment purchases financed under Loans 1471-CO and 2121-CO (for
about US$40 million) and under co-financing arrangements (for about US$60.0
million) substantially renewed 60% of the over-aged equipment fleet during the
years 1982 through 1986. Additional procurement of repair equipment and tools
(for about US$500,000) under loans 2121-CO and 2829-CO aided the equipment
rehabilitation capacity. The proposed project includes purchase of equipment
and further rehabilitation of existing equipment in function of the needs
arising from the road maintenance programs (annex 6).

COLOMBIA
THIRD NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
Highwav Maintenance
Hiahwavs Network
1.    The national highway system comprises some 26,000 km of roads under the
jurisdiction of MOPT.  The system consists of: (a) primary road network of
about 11,000 km that connects the main economic centers among themselves and
with the seaports and neighboring countries; and (b) about 15,000 km of
secondary roads that link smaller towns to the primary network and roads that
provide access to other transport modes (i.e. river ports) (Table 1). The
national highway network experienced rapid development until the 1980s, in
support of a growing and diversified economy. During the 1970-1986 period,
its size increased by some 30% (mainly due to the transfer of gravel and earth
roads from the departmental to the national network), while the length of the
paved network almost doubled, reflecting the effort made by MOPT in upgrading
the network. In 1988, over 500 km of roads in urban areas of the department
capitals were transferred to the corresponding municipalities and since then
the network has scarcely grown.
2.    From the mid-1970s onward, road maintenance requirements increased
progressively, reflecting the rapid expansion of the network and the aging of
its older sections. The increasing deterioration of the road network, and
that progressive consolidation of the primary highway system, require that the
highest priority be given to the rehabilitation and maintenance of existing
facilities. This would include the continuation and extension of road safety
and training programs initiated under the First and Second Highways Sector
projects. Moreover, investments in equipment and spare parts are required for
replacement and rehabilitation of obsolete road maintenance units (Annex 6).
Proarammina and Financino
3.    The MOPT highway program for 1991-95 has been prepared to address
identified road physical requirements as well as constraints to a more
effective highway management. MOPT's highway program would amount to an
estimated US$1.6 billion (including new road maintenance equipment and
rehabilitation of the existent one) throughout the 1992-1995 period, of which
road maintenance, including routine, periodic and emergencies maintenance
would account for 18%. We consider this figure adequate.
4.    The consolidated annual investment level for rehabilitation and periodic
maintenance throughout the 1992-1995 period, would be kept at about the same
level as in the 1983-1990 period. However, an increasing share would be
allocated to periodic maintenance, reflecting the considerable effort in
rehabilitation carried out in the 1980s. Investments in paving and road
safety would continue to increase gradually to keep pace with traffic growth,
while investments in equipment will decline after 1994, because of planned



- 77 -
ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 5
increases in maintenance works to be carried out by the private sector.
Expenditures in routine maintenance would increase noticeably in the two first
years of the proposed Project, to catch up with the backlog of 1989 and 1990,
and decrease moderately in the last two years of the Project, reflecting the
progressive exclusion of some activities traditionally considered as periodic
maintenance (i.e. construction of drainages, walls and overlays), which will
be included in the reconstruction or rehabilitation works for the various road
projects.
Maintenance Management
5.    Maintenance management is an area with a clear scope for improvement.
First, comprehensive maintenance planning has not been practiced so far. The
data for budget preparation have little input from the field operation units.
The budget format is very general and is not based on any measure of expected
work performance. Each year, the budget submission represents a similar or a
slightly increased amount over the previous year, but with little assessment
of what funds are really required for each maLntenance function. Second, the
release and allocation of funds continues to present a problem. The funds can
be allocated to carry out construction works and works on urban or rural roads
that do not belong to the MOPT network. The lack of the essential link
between planned work and actual funding makes fiscal management very
difficult. Third, there are delays in releasing of funds, which prevent the
districts from planning their work properly. Fourth, there is no clear
coordination between the different institutions involved in road maintenance.
In some cases there is duplication of field facilities and the specialized
labor and equipment is not efficiently used. The MOPT regional district
offices, each one staffed and possessing equipment, workshop facilities and
stocks of spare parts, operate in practically the same locations where FNCV's
regional offices are established. The departments, commissariats and
intendencies and a large number of local governments also haie field
facilities, spare parts, tools and equipment of their own. Fifth, there is no
adequate maintenance control, for lack of adequate information and monitoring
of the regional offices.
6.    Bank efforts under previous and the ongoing highway projects to improve
the efficiency of maintenance operations carried out by the districts have
been only partially successful, with low labor and equipmwnt productivity so
far preventing full achievement of agreed targets. Efforts have been made by
MOPT in the past to increa3e the perceatage or mainterance works carried out
by contract, which in 1987 was about 13% and at prasent is about 30%. Of
particular interest has been MOPT's innovative initiative to contract routine
maintenance with labor-intensive small cooperatives (micro-enterprises) of 11
to 15 workers covering about 50 km of road each. This program has been highly
successful and has demonstrated strong comparative advantage over routine
maintenanc. carried out by force account.  At present, there are 333 micro-
enterprises (covering about 18,000 km of primary roads) in operation. The
amount of contracted routine and periodic maintenance would be increased under
the proposed loan (Table 7), reaching, as an average in the 1992-95 period,



- 78 -
ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 5
33.1% wLth local contractors and 15.9% with small cooperatives (micro-
enterprises).
7.    As stated in Annex 1 para. 13, and shown in the table of that Annex, the
maintenance budgets remained stable in real terms until 1987, increasing in
1988 and decreased slightly in 1989 and 1990. Furthermore, the budget
approved for 1991 is below the needs estimated by the Maintenance Management
Unit for this year. These facts confirm the need to prepare adequate
maintenance programs and budgets and to establish an appropriate control.
8.    Improving maintenance planning, budgeting, and control, requires
implementation of the maintenance and equipment management systems, which were
partly carried out with funds provided under previous Bank loans. The systems
will require updating data concerning effective work force for maintenance
activities, list of equipment and its condition, and highway inventory by
physical characteristics. The implementation of the maintenance management
system will provide the means to improve the budget process. The maintenance
work program will establish requirements for labor, equipment and materials.
Consequently, for the first time, the Maintenance Management Unit will be able
to submit a highway maintenance budget based on specific types and quantities
of work to be accomplished.
Highwavs Maintenance Proarams (1991-1995)
9.    During the preparation of this Project, the parameters, guidelines and
handbooks left by the consultants who provided technical assistance in road
maintenance, and equipment and pavement management under loans 1487-co and
2121-co, together with the data collected by MOPT on costing and accounting
during the last years, were used by MOPT to prepare a provisional system for
planning purposes. Based on this, maintenance programs for the period 1991-
1995 were prepared.
10.   The first maintenance program studied was the program for 1991, for
which 33 maintenance activities were selected (12 activities for routine
maintenance, 18 activities for periodic maintenance and 3 for emergencies).
For each activity a work measurement unit (e.g. square meter) was assigned.
The level of maintenance service to be provided was established for what was
considered a full level of maintenance service. This "full level" was defined
by a set of quantity standards (e.g. square meters of patching per year) with
a separate standard for each activity. Quantity standards were fixed in
accordance with the existing experience. once maintenance activities were
selected and the levels of maintenance were expressed in quantity standards,
annual work quantities were determined for each maintenance activity by
multiplying the quantity standard (which quantifies the level of service), by
the appropriate inventory quantity. The amounts and types of resources
required were determined by using each maintenance activity's performance
standard. (These performance standards define the activity, the daily
resource requirements, the expected daily accomplishment and the approved work
method. They will also serve to monitor the execution of the programs and to
adjust future unit costs and daily accomplishment). Annual costs for each



- 79 -
ANNEX 2
Page 4 of 5
activity were determined by multiplying the resource quantities by unit costs
for labor, equipment and mater'.als as determined by the Maintenance Management
Unit (GAM). The sum of all annual activity costs is the maintenance budget.
11.   The network 1ength considered under maintenance in the 1991 program was
2C.657 km. corresponding to the length of the total highways network less
ibout 5,000 km under reconstruction or rehabilitation programs in 1991. The
maintenance work program and budget were prepared for each highway maintenance
sub-district and district. As a sum of these programs, a general program and
budget were prepared for the nations's entire highway network. The total
maintenance budget for 1991. as calculated, is US$77.7 million of which
US$41.9 million (54.0%) is for activities considered as routine maintenance,
US$30.9 million (39.7%) for activities considered as periodic maintenance, and
US$4-9 million (6.3%) for emergency works. Out of the total maintenance
program, us$38.7 million (49.8%) would be carried out by force account,
US$27.2 million (34.9%) by contract and US$11.8 million (15.2%) by micro-
enterprises (Table 2). Due to budget constraints, the final maintenance
budget for 1991 was US$72.4 million. The program for 1991 was a step forward
in improving road maintenance procedures and modifying current maintenance
practices. It set up a starting poLnt for developing the maintenance programs
for the following years, which will be included in the proposed Project, with
a commitment from the Government to allocate the funds required to carry out
these maintenance programs.
12.   The programs for 1992, 1993, 1994 and 1995 (Tables 3-6) were prepared on
the same basis that of the 1991 program. They include routine maintenance on
roads which are now under construction and will be completed by 1991. They
are also based on the assumption that a total of about 5,000 km will be
reconstructed or rehabilitated during the project period. It was estimated
that highways under reconstruction or rehabilitation in a specific year will
not need routine nor periodic maintenance in that year, but will need routine
maintenance in the followinq year and periodic maintenance after four years
from the time of the reconstruction or rehabilitation. A second assumption
was that some activities, so far considered as periodic maintenance, such as
construction of some drainage, walls and overlays, will be progressively
included in the investment projects for road rehabilitation and
reconstruction, reducing these activities in the maintenance programs to more
appropriate levels. A third assumption was that the equipment needed will be
available by acquiring new units to replace the old ones, rehabilitating other
existing units and repairing and maintaining adequately the rest. The
equipment to be acquired and the rehabilitation needed for the existing
oquipment will cost about US$30 million. Funding for acquisition and
rehabilitation of these units is included in this Project (Annex 6).
13.   The results (in USs) of the 1992-1995 maintenance programs are shown in
tables 3, 4, 5, and 6 dLvided among routine, periodic maintenance and
emergency works (to be carried out by force account, by contract and by micro-
enterprises). Table 7 shows a summary of these results. The total amount in
the period for routine maintenance is equivalent to us$170.3 million or an
average of US$1,648 per km per year; periodic maintenance is equivalent to



- 80 -
ANNEX 2
Page 5 of 5
us$95.6 million or Us$925 per km and the amount for emergency works is
equivalent tu us$20.4 million or US$198 per km. The total outlay per km is
US$2,771, which is conaidered satisfactory.
14.   The maintenance programs also define annual requirements in labor,
equipment and materials which are needed to carry them out. Based on these
data, MOPT's force account is not sufficient to carry out all the routine
maintenance, periodic mair,tenance and emergency works. Therefore, an
increasing share of road maintenance will have to be carried out by contract.
Tables 3 to 6 show the maintenance budget broken down into maintenance by
force account, maintenance by contract and maintenance by micro-enterprises.
The result is that about Us$94.7 million or 33.1% of the total maintenance
expenditures will be contracted to local contractors and about US$45.6 million
or 15.9% will be contracted to the labor-intensive small cooperatives (micro-
enterprises) with MOPT's financing. It is expected that the existing 333
micro-enterprises will cover annually over 20,000 km of primary and secondary
roads during the project period. The MOPT's equipment requirements to carry
out the maintenance programs are analyzed in Annex 6.
