Colombia
Third National Road Sector Proiect
River Navigation
A.   The River System
1.   Colombia has four river systems, the Magdalena, the Atrato, the Orinoco
and Amazonas, with a total of approximately 6,175 km of navigable waterway1'
and moving a total of approximately 2.7 million tons of cargo in 1989, plus
cattle and passengers. The Orinoco and Amazonas are of local importance,
representing together about 2Z of the national total, while the Atrato carries
a substantial volume of bananas (about 675 thousand tons in 1989) for
relatively short distances from the plantations to the port of Turbo for
loading to ocean-going ships.
2.   The most important river system for cargo movement is the Magdalena,
with 998 km of navigable waterway, and which in 1989 moved approximately 1.9
million tons of cargo (hydrocarbons 1.3 million tons, coal 0.3 million tons
and other cargo about 0.3 million tons) equivalent to about 1.1 billion ton-
km. River traffic on the Magdalena since 1971 is shown in Table 1. The
annual total over the period has remained remarkably steady; the decline in
general cargo in the 1960's, following the construction of the Atlantic
Railway in 1963, was compensated by the hydrocarbon led boom in the 1970's.
In its turn, the stagnation of the hydrocarbon traffic in the 1980's has been
compensated by the growth of coal movements. General cargo movements have
declined steadily over the period and now include relatively modest quantities
of cement (from Nare), fertilizers (from Barranquilla) and agricultural
products (from those communities in the lower Magdaleua where road
communications are non-existant). In part, the reasons for the stagnation of
the hydrocarbon traffic and the decline in general cargo are to be found in
the problem of the unreliability of river navigation; the project aims to
address the question of the present unreliability of river navigation.
Traffic prospects for the 1990's are positive for the bulk traffics,
especially petroleum products from Barrancabermeja and coal in the lower
reaches. Minor bulks, including fertilizers, cement and scrap may increase,
if reliability can be improved; other (general cargo) traffics will at best
maintain their present levels.
3.   River improvements between Barrancabermeja and La Gloria will benefit
mostly the movements ef petroleum products by ECOPETROL from Barrancabermeja
to the Caribbean ports and the long distance movements of fertilizers and
cement between Barranquilla and Puerto Berrio/Dorada. Improvements in the
Canal del Dique will benefit mostly the ECOPETROL traffic and coal traffic
from La Loma/Jagua to Cartagena.
1/   The figure refers to the length of waterway classified as "mayor
permanente" by the Directorate of River Transport and Ports (DGNP). The total
length of navigable waterway, including tributaries and sections subject to
seasonal closures is estimated to be 18,144 km.



- 89 -
Annex 3
Page 2 of 7
B.   Directorate of River Transport and Ports (DGNP)
(a) Organization
4.   Responsibilty for the maintenance of the river transport infrastructure
and for the regulation of river transport is held by the Directorate of River
Transport and Ports (DGNP), within the Ministry of Public Works and Transport
(MOPT). The Directorate is organized in three Divisions: Hydraulic Works,
which is numerically by far the largest, includes the sections responsible for
the dredgers and for river maintenance, and undertakes all the civil works of
the Directorate. Next in size is the Division of River Transport, which is
responsible for the regulation of the four river systems that make up the
Directorate. Third is the Technical Division, which is responsible for
studies, the hydraulic laboratory, for programming and control and for the
supervision of works. The overall organization of the Directorate is shown in
chart 1. The work of the hydraulic laboratory is performed by consulting
engineers under contract to the Directorate.
5.   In all, the Directorate employs about 655 personnel; of this total 186
are classified as staff and 469 as permanent workers. Those responsible for
the administration, operation and maintenance of the dredgers make up about
68% of all personnel and the Intendencias another 20%. Less than 10% of all
personnel are located in Bogota. The geographical distribution of the
Directorate is shown in Table 2.
6.   The turnover of staff at all levels is low so that, especially for
supervisory and management staff, there is likely to be a need for retraining
and the updating of skills. Such training is included under the proposed
project. Given the low availability of the dredgers, on account of
maintenance and supply problems, there is a significant degree of overmanning
in the Dredging Section.
(b)   Expenditure and Budget
7.   Budgeted expenditure for the Directorate for 1991 amounts to a total of
COL$ 8,499 million (US$ 13.5 million). This is, in real terms, in line with
average expenditure over the past four years (Table 3). The expenditure is
expected to finance: administrative costs (about 30%); studies, including the
two laboratories and the survey vessel (about 15%); the direct operating costs
of the dredgers and other equipment (about 20%); eleven contracts for the
protection of the river banks (about 30%, of which the largest single item
refers to the Canal del Dique) and minor works in approximately eight river
ports (about 15%). In the absence of more detailed figures, the allocation



- 90 -
Annex 3
Page 3 of 7
between the different headings shows no obvious distortions, though the
proportion for administrative costs appears high and the proportion for the
direct operating costs of the dredgers and other equipment is low, reflecting
their low availability.
8.   The proposed investment program for 1992-1995 is also shown in Table 3
and, without the Bank project, assumes a stable pattern of expenditure in real
terms.  The Directorate's expenditure is financed mainly from the Fondo Vial
(98%) and the remainder from Central Government.
(c)  DescriDtion of Proposed Investment
9.   The purpose of the proposed investment is to improve river transport in
the following ways:
a)   extending the period of navigability from Barrancabermeja to the
Atlantic coast from the present average of 9 months, to year-round
availability;
b)   improve the installations at the river port of Barrancabermeja, in
order to achieve more efficient cargo handling and to increase
capacity.
c)   extend the length of the working day for the convoys, by means of
the provision of improved river signalling.
10. Towards the achievement of the first objective, the extension of the
period of navigability, two works are envisaged; the partial training of the
river in the stretch between Barrancabermeja (km 631 from the sea) and La
Gloria (km 435) and a program of deferred maintenance dredging in the Canal
del Dique, which provides access from the river at Calamar (km 91)!' to the
bay and port of Cartagena (see map). A successful project will thus permit
year-round navigation between Barrancabermeja, where the ECOPETROL refinery is
situated, and the two outlets to the sea, at Barranquilla and at Cartagena.
Improvements to the river port of Barrancabermeja are essentially for dry
cargo, of which the port handled approximately 125 thousand ton in 1989 (about
35Z of the 1982 tonnage), and are intended to increase the speed of cargo
handling, thereby reducing the time needed to handle the traffic, reducing
congestion and increasing capacity. These cargoes have their origin or
destination in the sea ports of Cartagena and Barranquilla, where reasonably
adequate installation already exist. The aids to navigation consist
principally of reflecting buoys and markers. The total cost of the four
components is estimated to be US$ 26.6 million, made up as follows (Table 4):
2/   Distances are measured from the mouth of the river at Barranquilla; the
Canal del Dique has a total length of 114 km from the bay of Cartagena to
Calamar, where it joins the river.



- 91 -
Annex 3
Page 4 of 7
a)   river training and deepening:  Barrancabermeja-la Gloria, US$ 17.6
million
b)   dredging:  Canal del Dique, US$ 3.5 million
c)   improvements to river port: US$ 5.0 million
d)   aids to navigation : US$ 0.5 million
11. The content of each of the four components is described briefly below:
a)   River Training and Deei)eninm:  Barrancabermela - La Gloria
The River Magdalena is confined to a relatively wide valley by the
mountains on either side.  However, within the valley, movements of
the navigable channel occur with some frequency, on account of the
complex system of secondary channels. The purpose of this
component is, by a combination of channel deepening and the closing
of the secondary channels, to stabilize the main channel. In some
sections two main channels will be maintained. The deepening will
be achieved by dredging and the closing of the secondary channels
by the use of semipermeable barriers constructed of steel, aided by
the deposition of dredged material. The effect of the work will be
seen in the periods of low water, since in times of high water
there are no problems of navigation;
b)   Deferred Dredging of the Canal del Digue
Following Dutch studies in the 1960's and an IDB funded project in
the period 1979-83, three sediment traps have been established in
the Canal del Dique, with the aim to catch bottom-moving sediment,
thus maintaining the depth of the Canal. The project consists of
the cleaning of these traps, some dredging in the Canal between the
traps in order to restore the bottom profile and minor works to
stabilize the banks of the Canal. The two mouths of the Canal,
into the Bay of Cartagena and into the Bay of Barbacoas, will also
be dredged. The program is essentially one of deferred maintenance
dredging and in this case, as in the previous component, the
definition of satisfactory arrangements to ensure adequate
maintenance dredging after completion of the project will be a
condition of disbursement of the component;
c)   River Port Improvement
Intermodal links for the middle-Magdalena are provided by Capulco
(river and rail) and Barrancabermeja (river and road). The
installations at Capulco are in reasonably good condition,
requiring only minor repairs and, once some administrative details
related to the transfer of this port from the FNC (railway) to the
MOPT are resolved, it is planned to seek a concessionneire for the
port, who would take over both its operation and ma-intenance. The
port of Barrancabermeja is totally inadequate for present demand,



- 92 -
Annex 3
page 5 of 7
on account of its constrained location, between the installations
of ECOPETROL and the city. In addition, the construction of the
new highway (Troncal del Magdalena) will improve access to
Barrancamerja and may be expected further to increase demand there.
For this port, a two staged improvement is planned. First, the
capacity of the existing installations will be maximized, by
removing an unwanted warehouse and by increasing and paving the
storage yard; then to provide additional capacity, comprising
approximately 240m of quay with storage areas and accesses, at )ne
of two nearby locations currently being analysed. This will permit
improved cargo handling at the existing installation, with
additional capacity at an efficient new installation.
d) Aids to Navigation
There are at present few aids to navigation for the river, partly
on account of budgetry constraints, partly because of technical
difficulties in mapping the channel i' face of the relatively
frequent and rapid changes that it suffers. Conse'-uently there is
a high degree of reliance on the experience and knowledge of the
river captains, who nevertheless are obliged to reduce their speed
to a mintnum during periods of low water. One effect of the
planned works will be to stabilize the channel which, coupled with
the adoption of modern hydrographic equipment, will make the
installation of rixed navigation aids more feasible. This will
permit a longcr working day for the convoys, with a higher average
speed at time of low water. Fixed aids, consising primarily of
reflecting markers and some floating buoys, are envisaged.
D.   River Maintenance and Dredzers
12.   Constant river maintenance is necessary if navigation is to be reliable.
In the River Magdalena/Catal del Dique system, maintenance may be classified
into one of three categories, as either;
(a)   routine and predictable;
(b)   routine and predictable  within limits, or
(c)   emergency or unpredictable.
13.    The first category, of routine and predictable maintenance, is to be
found chiefly in the cleaning of the sediment traps in the Canal del Dique, to
catch the sediment before it settles in the channel, thus impeding navigation.
The second category, of the less predictable but still routine maintenance
dredging, is for the river itself and the river training component of the
project is designed in part to reduce this unpredictability by stabilizing the
navigation channel. The third category, emergency work, refers to the sudden
and unpredictable shoaling that arises from time to time. This is always a
possibility in a river that retains most of its natural characteristics and
some reserve of dredging capacity has to be maintained for this eventuality.



- 93 -
Annex 3
Page 6 of 7
14.   River maintenance has been inadequate in recent years and this, coupled
with a period of less than average rainfall, has led to serious problems of
navigation. The maintenance probiem arises from an insufficiency of dredging
capacity in the country for this type of work (which, by reason of its
fragmented nature is not likely to attract international interest). The MOPT
has traditionally done the work using its own equipment and, on account of
this policy, the development of a private sector capability has been
discouraged. The Directorate owns what is, on paper, a substantial fleet of 9
dredgers. However, the most recent of these was purchased in 1976, another
was purchased in 1965 and the rest prior to 1960 (Table 5). Thus on account
of their age and associated maintenance and spare parts problems, the
availability of t'^e dredgers is poor. Their performance is further affected
by deficiencies in the supply of dredging pipe and by the inherent
difficulties in maximizing hours of work in the context of a state-owned
dredging fleet.
15.   Some initial steps have been taken to supplement the fleet by the
coatracting of private dredgers, of which a limited number now exists, and
also under -onsideration is the possTlhility of chartering out the
Directorate"s dredgers on "dry-boat" terms, i.e. without crew, for specific
works, though their condition may hinder implementation of this policy.
Another possibility, at least for the medium term, is to irclude an option of
maintenance dredging for a period of say 5 years in the contracts for the
works in the river and in the Canal del Dique that are to be financed under
the project.
16.   Approval, by the Bank, of the contracts for the project components
related to dredging in the river and in the Canal del Dique will depend on
prior agreement with the MOPT of the arrangements for the essential
maintenance dredging that is invol ed. This will require the careful
definition and evaluation of the options open for the achievement of the
estimated dredging need3', involving an analysis of both technical and
institutional/laboral aspects. Provision has be - made in the project for the
cost of a study to examine these issues and to recommend the best solution.
The terms of reference are attached as .....
E.   Tariffs
17.   River users pay a charge to the MOPT for use of the river and, when use
is made of a public port, for use of the port and hire of equipment (if
applicable). In addition, river transport loading or discharging in the ports
3I    Made up of routine maintenance of a largely predictable nature plus the
emergency work arising from unexpected changes in river conditions.



- 94 -
Annex 3
Page 7 of 7
of Cartagena and Barranquilla is liable for a charge by COLPUERTOS. The two
tariffs have not been updated since 1988 and 1984 respectively and the
incidence of the charges is very low.!'  In 1990 the MOPT received a total of
COL$ 41.3 million (US$ 0.1 million) from river transport through the tariff
(Table 6). In principle, the tariff should cover at least the cost of the
maintenance of the river channel and the operating cost of any equipment,
including provision for depreciation.  For the project, the MOPT will prepare
by June 30, 1992, an adequate tariff structure in line with these objectives,
and will implement the new tariff by the end of 1992.
F.   Training ComRonent and Studies
18.   Two items are envisaged under this heading:
a)    the development and implementation of a program for the training
of DNP staff in all aspects of the administration of navigable
river systems. It is suggested that this program be developed in
cooperation with the US Corps of Engineers station at Viksburg,
though taking into consideration the need for a suitably
commercial approach to cost recovery and tariff aspects. An
outline of the proposed training program is given in Annex 8;
b)    a study of the options open to the MOPT for the successful
accomplishment of the necessary river maintenance, in light of
expected demand for predictable and for emergency work. This will
involve in particular the definition and evaluation of the
alternatives for the dredgers and associated equipment of
contracting, leasing or direct operation.
A/    The charge for use of the river is between Col$0.02 and Col$0.04 per
ton-kn, depending on the nature of the cargo, whilst the hire of equipment
(cranes or forklift trucks) costs between Col$ 1000 and Col$ 2500 per hour,
depending on the capacity of the equipment.

COLOMBIA
THIRD NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
Bank Assistance to the Hiahway Subsector
A.    Highway Proiects
(i)   Completed Proiects
1.    From the early 1950s to the mid-1970s, the Bank Group contributed to the
development of the highway subsector through six loans and one credit, which
assisted in the expansion of the primary network and in the introduction of
modern engineering and construction techniques. The first generation of
highway projects (Loan 43-CC,- is$16.5 million, 1951; Loan 84-:o, us$14.35
million, 1953: Loan 144-Co, US$16.5 million, 1956; Loan 295-co, us$19.5
million, and Credit 05-Co us$19.5 million, 1961; Loan 550-Co, us$17.2 million,
1968; and Loan 680-CO, us$32.0 million, 1970) eventually resulted in the
construction or upgrading of 4,500 km of trunk roads, which constitute the
backbone of Colombia's two south-north corridors to the Atlantic ports, their
interconnections and their link to the main Pacific port. The two last loans
also financed construction of one major bridge each. A major technical
assistance component financed under Loans 295-co and 550-Co, assisted in the
reorganization of MOPT and of the districts, adding transportation functions
and strengthening road administration.
2.    Progress of all of these projects was slow because of: (a) inadequate
designs; (b) awarding of contracts to inexperienced and financially weak local
firms (affecting mostly the two last projects); (c) scarcity of counterpart
funds; and (d) poor supervision and contract management. The resulting cost
overruns of up to 70% brought about reduction of the original projects scope
with unfinished parts of preceding loans being completed under subsequent
loans. Almost all loans required extensions of up to three years of their
closing dates to permit full disbursement of the loan proceeds.
3.    Although these six loans contributed fundamentallSy to the development of
Colombia's trunk system and to the emergence of road transport as the main
means of communication, the original objectives were only partially achieved
and the ultimate objective of the paving program to improve communications
between the Atlantic ports and the major commercial and industrial centers of
the country was not fully met. Nevertheless, the roads and bridges built with
Bank assistance are benefiting the Colombian economy by reducing vehicle
operating costs on important trade routes and, with a few exception, are
yielding high economic returns. The completion report of the Sixth Highway
Project (Loan 680-CO) recommended that: (a) MOPT's competence and capacity be
assessed more realistically; (b) engineering works be completed prior to loan
negotiations; and (c) the extreme importance of road maintenance be
recognized.
4.    The Seventh Highway Project (Loan 1471-Co, US$90 million, 1977) shifted
the Bank's involvement in the highway subsector to road rehabilitation and
maintenance and related institutional development, comprising: (a)
rehabilitation of about 978 km of paved trunk roads; (b) preparation and



- 103 -                 ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 7
implementation of a vehicle weight program; (c) a highway maintenance program
(1978-1982) focused on the ienewal and better management of MOPT's equipment
fleet and on the introduction of upgraded planning and management methods and
procedures for maintenance activities; and (d) updating of the national
transport plan and preparation of an inventory of local road materials.
5.    Project start-up was delayed for two years because of the changes in
administration (August 1978) and subsequent inquiries into MOPT's practices,
which paralyzed most procurement actions until February 1979. The new
administration had difficulties in reorganizing MOPT and restarting the
project. Fey consultants, in particular for maintenance planning and
equipment management, became involved only in early 1980. Some of the civil
works contracted during the first two years encountered administrative and
financial problems and failed; new contracts had to be negotiated or tendered.
It was not until the end of 1979 that project execution really got under way
with the full support of MOPT; however, implementation suffered new delays and
cost increases because of a combination of: (a) increases in quantities due to
the nature of the works and the additional deterioration between the time when
engineering was completed back in 1974 and the time of construction; and (b)
inflation, which was higher than anticipated over a longer period than
estimated. In October 1983, MOPT and the Sank agreed on a reduction of the
original scope of road rehabilitation from 974 km to 777 km, which were
completed by the end of 1985. The remaining 197 km of roads that were
initiated under the Seventh Highway Project were completed under the First
Highway Sector Project (Loan 2121-Co).
6.    The technical assistance for highway maintenance by consultants
INGEROUTE (France), was completed in 1982 and initiated and developed
implementation of systems for: (a) pavement MLnagement; (b) equipment and
spare parts management; and (c) training. After the departure of the
consultants, implementation of these initiatives slowed down, but received
some impetus under the Government that took over in August 1986. Maintenance,
road marking and laboratory equipment for a total of US$24.0 million was
procured under the project. In addition, MOPT procured maintenance equipment
for about US$60 million with bilateral credits under a program set up under
the equipment management system. Expenditures for road maintenance increased
in real terms by about 50% in 1979 and 5% annually until 1983. The commitment
to keep maintenance labor and administration expenditures below a 50%
threshold of total maintenance expenditures was complied with, if amortization
of maintenance equipment is included in the calculation.
7.    Local consultants (TECNOCONSULTA LTDA.) completed a study in 1982 on the
economic, technical, financial and legal aspects of vehicle weight control and
presented their recommendations on the number, type and location of vehicle
weight control units, which were approved by the Bank. MOPT's cumbersome
budgeting and procurement procedures delayed procurement of weighing equipment
and tendering failed two times, in 1984 and 1985. Weighing equipment could
not be procured before the loan's closing date, December 31, 1985, and an
amount of US$2,173,923.00 in uncommitted funds was canceled. (Five portable
scales were procured subsequently under Loan 2121-co. and five stationary
weighing stations under Loan 2829-CO).
8.    The National Transport Plan was finalized and discussed with the Bank by
mid-1980 and led to the preparation of the First Highways Sector Project (Loan



- 104 -                ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 7
2121-co). Three contracts for the study and inventory of locally available
road materials were awarded in 1981 and 1982 to three local firms and were
completed in 1984. (This study was updated under the on-going Second Highway
Sector Project, Loan 2829-CO).  Although some of the project's objectives were
not fully achieved, the Seventh Highway Project contributed fundamentally to
the updating and strengthening of MOPT's administr&tive procedures and to the
improvemert of Colombia's primary road network, thus benefiting the economy by
substantial savings in transportation costs.
9.    The First Highway Sector project (Loan 2121-co), Us$152.3 million 1982)
aimed at financing MOPT's 1982-1986 Highway Program, including road
rehabilitation (3,700 km); paving (2,800 km); resealing (2,500 km); and new
construction (500 km).   It also aimed at improving subsector management
efficiency and strengthening MOPT's, highway planning and implementation
capability. Key measures included: (a) development of pluriannual highway
programs; (b) systematic use of economic appraisal me;nodologies in support of
program preparation; (c) introduction of a comprehensive road safety program;
(d) improvement of MOPT's management data system and computerization of
technical and financial records; (e) increasing periodic maintenance by
contract and rationalization of the force account organization; (f)
reclassification of the network and development of departmental highway plans;
and (g) improving of vehicle-axle-load control. Moreover, sector studies were
to be carried out under the project covering the National Transport Plan, road
user charges and truck regulation and services.
10.   The civil works program was to be financed jointly by MOPT's own funds,
by the First Highways Sector Project and by two IDB loans. However, financing
under the sector project was to be applied to the execution of specific
projects to be selected by stringent economic criteria. By the time of
negotiations, about 1,400 km of rehabilitation works and 700 km of paving
works had been preselected and evaluated, engineering was well advanced, 655
km of rehabilitation works had already been awarded and 148 km of paving works
were to be awarded. Fast startup of project implementation was expected.
However, the change in Government that took place one month before the date of
effectiveness of the Loan (9/6/82) delayed startup of project execution by
more than a year, and contracting of the awarded works was delayed until after
publication of a new national procurement law (Decree 222) in February 1983,
which introduced additional complications into the tendering and contracting
procedures. Several successful bidders refused to sign their contracts, which
brought about extended negotiations to award to the next lowest bidders.
Additional difficulties appeared when MOPT started to use the new bidding
procedures, which were not in accordance with the Bank Guidelines. This
problem further slowed down contracting of civil works and goods procurement,
and finally led to the cancellation of US$2.7 million of the amount of the
loan on May 3, 1984. The difficulties on the procurement issue were finally
solved in June 1984 and full project implementatinn got under way. In October
1983, the Bank had agreed to transfer from Loan lf71'-CO the completion of
execution of 197 km of road rehabilitation. An agreement on the opening of a
special account was signed on May 21, 1984. Nevertheless, execution of civil
works suffered new delays because of: (a) delays in contracting; (b) lack of
counterpart funding; (c) poor performance by contractors; and (d) weak
supervision. Following a general agreement with the Government, the Bank,
under the Special Action Program, increased disbursements for civil works from
50% to 65% from January 1, 1985 until June 30, 1986. An extension of the loan



- 105 -                ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 7
closing date from December 31, 1986 to December 31, 1987 wao agreed on May 26,
1985 and, finally, on June 19, 1986, the Bank agreed to extend 65% financing
of a few subprojects to December 31, 1986 and to apply 65% financing to the
execution of emergency civil works (Nevado del Ruiz) until their conclusion.
11.   Under the prcject MOPT: (a, carried out and partially implemented
several studies on policies in the transport sector and on upgrading road
specifications and technologies; (b) procured a mainframe computer and
initiated implementation of a computerized information system; (c) carried out
partially a program of institutional reform and training; (d) carried out
partially a road rehabilitation and paving program and a road safety program;
and (e) implemented a cost-effective program for contracting routine road
maintenance. Overall, however, the project wae not fully successful in
meeting its objectives: (a) MOPT's 1982-1986 five-year program exparienced a
14% reduction in real terms, affecting mainly such project-related components
as training (-63%), paving (-55%), road safety (-41%) and maintenance (-20%);
(b) MOPT failed to achieve m.,ny of its institutional strengthening objectives,
including: (i) efficient budget planning and administration; (ii)  effective
maintenance administration; (iii) adequate vehicle weight control; and (iv)
upgrading training programs; and (c) the project had no effect on
strengthening the construction industry.
12.   Full implementation of the project was hampered by: (a) lack of
sustained commitment of the government to the objectives of the project; (b)
frequent changes in high- and mid-level staff assignment; (c) defective
engineering and inadequate cost estimates; (d) cumbersome bidding and
contracting procedures; (e) lack of consistency between budgetary and civil
works execution programming, resulting in a chronic shortage of funds to
finance the civil works program, extreme delays in processing of payments, and
substantial ixtensions of the original schedule for execution of civil works;
(f) weak pe:;formance of most contractors and extremely costly and ineffective
supervision by consultants; (g) MOPT's failure to implement maintenance and
equipment management systems introdu-ed by consultants under Loan 1471-co, to
establish adequate maintenance information and cost-accounting systems and to
strengthen maintenance planning, execution and monitoring.
(ii)  Oncoina Proiect (Loan 2829-co)
13.   The Second Highway Sector Project (Loan 2829-co, US$180.3 million, 1987)
consists of an investment component and a policy reform and institutional
development action plan. The investment component finances a time-slice of
MOPT's 1987-1993 highway program and the loan disburses against selected
expenditures, including: (a) civil works for rehabilitation of paved and
gravel roads; (b) supervision of the corresponding road rehabilitation and
paving works; (c) road safety; (d) equipment for road maintenance and spare
parts; (e) training program; and (f) technical assistance. The Action Plan
was designed to strengthen MOPT's highway management capabilities, adjust
sector policies and improve the transport coordination process through the
implementation of programming and monitoring systems for the different
subsector agencies.
14.   The loan finances contracts which were ittarted either with funding from
the previous loan (Loan 2121-co), or wLth MOPT's own funding. The main reason
for this financing is that, for years, MOPT has held a large portfolio of



- 106 -                ANNEX 4
Page 5 of 7
legally valid contracts without sufficient resources to sustain their adequate
implementation. This has resulted in slow progress and high costs. In
addition, deficient engineering resulted in continuous modifications during
construction adding to the delays and cost overruns. Inadequate selection of
contractors added to this problem. Problems in execution are analyzed for the
sub-projects financed by Loan 2829-Co, contract by contract, in Annex 5. On
July 15, 1988, an amendment to the Loan Agreement increased the Bank loan's
share in financing the costs of civil works and their supervision, in order to
complete the contracts in a reasonable period. At the same time, MOPT
confirmad that it would not start new civil works, except for emergencies and
maintenance, and updated the work program. However, the extremely long
process to include the additional loan funds in the 1988 budget precluded
achieving the physical objectives for 1988. MOPT updated the work program
once again in late April 1989, taking into account the time required to secure
the budget revisions and the authorization from the Ministry of Finance to
spend funds. This work program 4ncl,ded 160 contracts. As of that date only
7.4% of the loan disbursements had been processed by the Bank and up to 14%
were awaiting Bank processing.
15.   Project implementation has substantially improved in the last year,
especially for the civil works component. The main reasons for this
improvement have been adequate allocation of funds in the budget and better
management of the budget process. Disbursements as of December 31, 1989 were
US$81.0 million compared to US$13.4 million as of end-1988, and length of
finished roads was 1971 km (326 km in end-1988). However, the number of
legally valid contracts remained very high (228, of which 68 were finished by
the end of 1990). This large number of contracts could not be appropriately
funded and slowed progress on all works.
16.   Project progress continued in 1990; as of the end of 1990 only US$37.3
million remained undisbursed (20.7% of the Loan), well ahead of the schedule
given in the SAR (34% of the Loan). The Loan is now exp--ted to be totally
disbursed by Dec-imber 31, 1991, one year ahead of the cl.  ng date eptablished
in the Loan Agreement.
17.   Although almost all civil works and the corresponding supervision are
already contracted, it will not be possible to finance ongoing works through
completion with this loan. About us$140 million will be needed to complete
ongoing civil works.
18.   Disbursements In the training component and scholarships have started
very slowly but picked up in the last year. The Bank agreed with MOPT to
allocate funds from the Loan to the following: (i) implementing maintenance
planning and control as well as new technical guidelines for road design and
construction; (ii) training for equipment operators to improve the present
quality of equipment operation and maintenance; and (iii) a program of seminar
and courses. Of the technical assistance component, at present only the
Institutional Management Study has been awarded. Proposals submitted by
consultants to prepare a National Transport Plan and to review the impact of
truck manufacturing policies were declared void by MOPT for legal reasons.
The Master Plan Study is now being retendered but the trucking study will not
be carried out because it became unnecessary given the study carried out by
the Ministry of Economic Development and the recent liberalization of imports
of vehicles.



- 107 -                 ANNEX 4
Page 6 of 7
B.    Rural Roads Projects
19.   The Rural Roads Project (Loan 1966-Co, US$33.0 million, 1981) aimed at
strengthening the planning and managerial capability of the National Rural
Roads Fund (FNCV) and to develop this organization Into an efficient planning
and funding agency for rural roads programs carried out by its departmental
branches. The project assisted FNCV in carrying out its 1981-1984 investment
plan and maintenance plan, and provided technical assistance to upgrade the
management of the institution.
20.   After an initial delay of several months, execution of the project
became satisfactory. A substantial decrease of the estimated road
construction and rehabilitation cost allowed an increase of about 500 km of
the length of roads financed under the project. However, successive
extensions of the closing date were required from June 30, 1984 to December
31, 1986. Civil works execution and procurement of maintenance equipment were
completed in 1985. Road maintenance was not totally performed in compliance
with agreed targets. All technical assistance services were completed and
'ontributed to FNCV's institutional strengthening.
21.   The Rural Transport Project (Loan 2668-co, Us$62.0 million, 1986) was
prepared based on the results achieved under the Rural Roads Project and
supports FNCV's 1986-1992 Investment and Maintenance Program. It finances a
slice of this program and aims at: (a) supporting the Government's effort in
agricultural development; (b) improving efficiency and reducing cost of rural
transport; (c) upgrading FNCV's institutional performance; and (d) developing,
on a pilot basis, small scale water transportation in a coastal area. This
project is giving particular attention to the execution of adequate rural
roads maintenance programs. Execution of this project started in the third
quarter of 1986. The closing date is December 31, 1992.
22.   Progress of the road construction, rehabilitation and periodic
maintenance are ahead of expectations. Funds appropriated by the Government
for these purposes have permitted to speed up the tendering, contracting and
execution. Consulting services for technical asuistance are practically
completed. Implementation of the maintenance management system is going on
and contributing to stepped-up management performance. If the level of
budgetary support continues to be satisfactory next year, the loan will be
fully disbursed in 1991, one year ahead of the closing date.
23.   The Second Rural Roads Sector Project (Loan 3157-co, US$55.0 million,
1990) is based on FNCV's 1990-1993 Investment and Maintenance Program. The
project embodies the experience gained in preparing and implementing the
program for the 1986-1989 period, and, because it is based on annually
reviewed physical targets, expenditures and financing levels (chosen in
accordance with agreed methods and criteria), provides the flexibility to
adapt to changing developmental priorities, especially now that coordination
with local governments is mandated under the new decentralization scheme. The
main project components are: (a) maintenance, including resurfacing works on
4,400 km of roads; (b) improvement civil works along 1,500 km of existing
dry-weather roads, including completion of bridges and drainage structures;
(c) construction of 900 km of rural roads; (d) studies including the review of
road funding strategies and engineering designs, and supervision of civil



- 108 -                 ANNEX 4
page 7 of 7
works; and (e) training, technical assistance and other services. The project
was approved by the Board on January 16, 1990 and declared effective on June
6. 1990. The closing date is December 31, 1995.
24.   Execution of the project started soon after declaration of loan
effectiveneas and some civil works and the corresponding supervision have been
contracted and initiated. Procurement of goods is progressing satisfactorily.
The implementation of.the maintenance management system, successfully
initiated under the Loan 2668-Co, is being extended country-wide with the
assistance of consultants financed by the new project. Project implementation
is progressing satisfactorily.

COLOMBIA
THIRD NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
Draft Contract between the Ministry of Public Works and Transport
(National Road Fund) the and Fiscal Policy Council:
Monitoring of the Performance of the National Road Fund
Preamble
1.        The Fiscal Policy council, Ministry of Public Works and Transport
(MOPT), Ministry of Finance and Public Credit, and the National Planning
Department (DNP) are interested in improving the administration of the
National Highway Fund (FVN) in order to achieve the goals of MOPT's Road Plan
for the ADertura (DNP-UNINF-DITRANS-MOPT-2552 dated March 21, 1991) and the
program for restructuring of MOPT.
2.        Negotiations are currently in progress with the World Bank to
obtain financing for the country's Third National Roads Sector Project.
Finalization of such negotiations is contingent upon introduction of efficient
mechanisms for monitoring the administration of the National Highway Fund and
its execution of investment projects.
Parties to the Contract
3.        Juan Felipe Gaviria Gutierrez, Minister of Public Works and
Transport, legal representative of the National Highway Fund; Rudolf Hommes,
Minister of Finance and Public Credit; and Armando Montenegro, Director,
National Planning Department.
General Oblective
4.        To improve the programming and execution of projects implemented by
the National Highway Fund, improve road maintenance practices and introduce
necessary institutional improvements to upgrade quality of project management
and transport policy setting.
S2ecific Goals
5.   (a)  To set the level of commitments of the National Road Fund in line
with its financial resources;
(b) To rationalize administrative procedures and reduce costs;
(c) To improve investment programming and monitoring; and
(d) To enable efficient execution of the Third National Roads Sector
Project financed by the IBRD.



- 110 -
ANENE 5_
Page 2 of 3
Undertakings
6.        The National Road Fund undertakes to:
(a) Limit contractual commitments for civil works so that the ratio of
total revenues of FVN in each year divided by total outstanding
contracts will be at least equal to the following;
1992      252
1993      302
1994      402
1995      502
(b) execute the 1992-95 investment program (desegregated by project),
as per attachment 2.
(c) Execute the 1992-95 road maintenance plan (desegregated by region),
as per attachment 3.
(d) Carry out the institutional improvement program as per attachment
4.
(e) Prepare each year an updated program specifying quarterly targets
for implementation of investment projects, road maintenance and
studies.
(f) Execute at least 80% of the quarterly plan for each investment
project, road maintenance plan by region and sector studies.
(g) Take, before December 31, 1992, all necessary actions to complete a
reform of the Statutory Law of FVN which will include
reorganization of the maintenance functions of MOPT and the
incorporation of the Project Executing Unit and the Environmental
Unit into its organization structure.
7.        The Fiscal Policy Council undertakes to:
(a) Ensure that approval of the budget requests within its jurisdiction
is consistent with National Highway Fund financial programming.
(b) Approve the PAC (Plan annual de Caia) required for the National
Road Fund.
(c) Contract consultants to monitor this contract. The selected firm
will prepare the progress reports in line with the guidelines in
attachment 1.
(d) Evaluate, in conjunction with the National Road Fund, and based on
the consultants' reports, the progress in execution of the Third
National Roads Sector Project and recommend the necessary
corrective measures.



- 111 -
ANNEX 5
Page 3 of 3
8.        The National Planning Departments commits itself to communicate to
CONFIS its opinion on MOPT's funding requests within 10 working days.
9.        The Ministry of Finance, through its General Directorate of Budget,
commits itself to:
(i)       have the Directorate General of the Budget respond within
10 working days to budget requests referred to it by
MOPT; and
(ii)      agree with MOPT on the proposals for amendments to the
regulations issued pursuant to Law 38 of 1989, within
three months of the signing of this document.
Follow-up and Loan Monitoring
10.       The Fiscal Policy Council, in cciajunction with MOPT and the
National Planning Department, shall utilize the auditors' reports as the basis
for evaluating overall administration of the loan in relation to physical and
financial goals and shall recommend actions to be taken iu cases of poor
performance.
11.       Projects on which at least 80% of programmed activities have not
been carried out shall be studied with a view to specific actions, as allowed
by the statute on contracting. Projects whose costs exceed the estimated
costs by over 20% will also be reviewed to find out the reason for such
increase.  In both cases, CONFIS will recommend the actions to be taker. to
remedy the situation.
Duration
12.       This contract shall take effect on the date of its signing and
publication in the Diario Oficial and will have a duration through the end of
1995. Annually, through agreement by all parties to this contract, and with
the participation of the World Bank, the targets established in this document
could be modified in light of existing circumstances and new quarterly targets
agreed upon.
Submission of Reports
13.       The reports will be submitted no later than 30 days after the
quarter in question.
Distribution of Reports
14.       Progress reports shall be forwarded by MOPT's Planing Office to the
Secretariat of the Fiscal Policy Council, with copies to the National Planning
Department, the World Bank Resident Representative in Colombia and the World
Bank headquarters.

Institutional Actions
MOPT shall carry out each of the fol3owing actionst
1.   Complete, not later than December 31, 1992, the document on road
technical specifications under Part B.1 (e) (1) of the Project.
2. Complete, not later than December 31, 1992, th& document on road
geometric design standards under Part B.1 (e) (2) of the Project.
3. Complete, not later than June 30, 1993, the manual on engineering norms
and guidelines under Part B.1 (e) (3) of the project.
4. Adopt new pre-qualification guidelines for civil works bidding, not
later than July 31, 1992.
S.   Complete the civil works supervision manual under Part B.1 (c) of the
project not later than June 30, 1993.
6.   Carry out an economic analysis of the national road network not later
than December 31, 1992.
7.   Install automatic traffic counters in, at lest, thirty percent (302) of
all toll stations by December 31, 1992; fifty percent (50X) of all toll
stations by December 31, 1993; seventy-five percent (75X) of all toll stations
by December 31, 1994; and in all toll stations by December 31, 1995.
8.   Put into operation five sets of portable weighing scales by not later
than December 31, 1992 and eight additional sets of portable weighing scales
by not later than December 31, 1993.
9.   Employ, not later than September 30, 1992, and thereafter maintain, a
full-time international trade specialist for purposes of coordinating all
training actOvities under the Project related to internodal transport
facilitation.
10. Prepare not later than June 30, 1993, a plan of action to facilitate
international transport procedures and regulations.
11. Complete the pavement design guide under Part B.1 (b) of the Project not
later than June 30, 1993.
12. Establish and make operational not later than June 30, 1993, the
computerized management information system under Part B.1 (d) of the Project.

ANNEX 6

COLOMB IA
'JIRQ NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJEC
Road Maintenance Ecquinnent
Eguipment Fleet
1.    The current MOPT road maintenance fleet is composed of 3833 units, of
which 290 (8%) are less than 5 years old; 1782 (46%) between 5 and 10 years
old; 370 (10%) 10 to 15 years old, and 1391 (36%) more than 15 years old. At
present, 1760 units are in service without problems, 777 units in service with
some problems, 483 units need minor repairs and 813 need rehabilitation or
reconstruction. Table 1 shows the various equipment groups and the current
condition of each group. As a result of the road maintenance programs for the
1992-95 period, (Annex 2, Tables 3, 4, 5 and 6) this fleet is not adequate to
carry out the maintenance work fy force account. New units will be needed to
complete or replace old units and many of the existing units between 5 and 10
years old will need major repairs, rehabilitation or reconstruction. Finally,
old units more than 10 years old will be sold at auction or discarded (see
Table 2).
EauiDment Manaaement
2.    At present, equipment management is not satisfactory.  Some units are in
poor condition because they are not properly operated and maintained. MOPT's
capital investment in equipment must be better protected by observing regular
preventive maintenance. The first steps in this direction were taken under
the technical assistance financed under Loan 2121-co and continued under Loan
2829-CO, now in its last phase. The existing structure for equipment
administration is adequate and only minor changes are needed. But, as stated
in Annex 1, para. 3, the coordination between the existing Sub-directorate of
Equipment, the Maintenance Management Unit (GAM) and the Districts must be
enhanced, while a new Road Maintenance Directorate (comprising all the units
related to maintenance) is created and organized.
3.    In the future, each equipment unit should preferably be assigned to a
specific operator who will be responsible for its operation and maintenance.
These operators should be trained under a program developed by the MOPT. Due
to the lack of tenure in operator positions, the program rhould be permanent.
The training will serve to improve the present quality of operation and
maintenance. This Project will finance the proposed training program (see
Annex 8).
4.    Furthermore, the equipment management system initiated with technical
assistance under Loan 2121-CO must be completed and implemented to match the
program for equipment rehabilitation, repairs and maintenance with the real



- 121 -
ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 2
needs arising from the highways maintenance programs. A component to finance
the completion and implementation of the maintenance and equipment management
system throughout the country is included in the project at a cost of
us$850,000 over a period of two years. Annex 7 contaLns the proposed terms of
reference for this technical assistance.
Ecuinment Accuisition and Rehabilitation
5.    In accordance with che resources needed to carry out the maintenance
programs for 1992-95 (Annex 2, Tables 3, 4, 5 and 6) a total of 753 new units
will be acquired in 1992-94 (see Table 3) at a cost of about US$25 million,
and will be financed by the proposed Project. Twenty five additional units
will be acquired with MOPT own funds (see Table 2).
6.    Furthermore, the units between 5 and 10 years old and some units between
10 and 15 years old will be rehabilitated during the same period, in
accordance with the program shown in Table 4. This program was prepared by
MOPT's Equipment Subdirectorate and includes the needs for the rehabilitation
and major repairs of all the existing units which will not be discarded (i.e.
those whose cost of rehabilitation is less than 40% of their replacement
cost).
7.    The equipment rehabilitation program will be carried out in MOPT's
workshops, which have proper too's, workshop equipment and competent
personnel. The proposed Project will finance US$5 million in spare parts
needed to rehabilitate the equipment listed in Table 4.
Eaui2ment Operational Costs and RJRair
8.    During the project period, large resources will be needed to operate the
equipment for the road maintenance programs. Table 5 shows a summary of the
equipment requirements for fuel, lut-icants, tires and spare parts to make
MOPT's equipment available and operd-ional in the period 1992-95.  These
figures coincide with the requirements for equipment in the maintenance
programs (see Tables 3 to 6 of Annex 2). To ensure that sufficient funds are
available, MOPT will include in its road maintenance budgets for 1992-95, the
amount in pesos equivalent to the amounts in USS shown for each year in Table
5. Close supervision will be needed to monitor allocated resources and to
ensure that they are applied effectively to achieve the equipment availability
and utilization targets. Maintenance and minor repairs of the road equipment
will be carried out by MOPT's workshops which have adequate facilities for
this purpose.

ANNEX 7

COLOMBIA
THIRD NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
Technical Assistance Component
1. Technical Assistance Under Previous Loans.
1.01     Provisions in Loan 2121-CO included the execution of special studies
for: i) continuous upgrading of the National Transporr- Plan including port
development policies, containerization and development of intermodal services
and analyses of freight transport; ii) reviewing trucking regulations and
services, including user charges; and iii) upgrading of road construction
technology and highway administration, including introduction of alternative
construction materials and asphalt emulsion technology, creation of a highway
data bank, and development of road design and evaluation techniques. The
National Transport Plan was submitted in 1984 and updated in 1986. The
studies of Road User Charges and of Transport Regulation and Trade
Facilitation were carried out with major delays and their implementation was
mostly postponed to be carried out under Loan 2829-Co. Of the four studies to
upgrade road technology and highway administration only the one referring to
bituminous emulsions was completed, but its practical application has not been
adequately implemented. The other three studies on slopes stability, use of
alternative road materials and upgrading technical specifications and
guidelines, were to be continued under the follow-up project because
consultant services were deployed too late to allow completion of their
assignments under the project.
1.02     The follow-up Loan 2829-CO was specifically designed to assist the
Government and MOPT in addressing pending issues. A comprehensive action plan
and a technical assistance program were outlined. The scope of this program
covered road transport regulation, investment planning, budget programming and
execution and highway administration. The following table shows a list of the
studies included under the Technical Assistance Program component, their main
objectives and to what degree these objectives have been achieved. Of the ten
studies, two were completed although with some variation from their original
objectives, three are still on-going, and the remaining five were discarded
because other Government agencies carried out similar studies (two cases) or
because of lack of conviction and commitment on the part of MOPT. There are
substantial variances in the success of the different studies carried out,
both in terms of the quality of the documents produced and in the degree of
implementation they achieved.

Page 2 of 8

2. The Proposed Proiect'e Technical Assistance component.
A. Background.
2.01         The technical assistance component, and the implementation of its
recommendations, would contribute to remove important bottlenecks to a more
effective sector and subsector management. The program would concentrate on
key issues identified during project preparation, putting special emphasis on
the implementation phase. In this context, the program would include
reviewing some of the studies carried out in the past to upgrade their content
and recommendations whenever necessary and to consistently implement such
recommendations. Consultants would assist and lead task forces designated
within MOPT to produce some of the technical and managerial mechanisms needed
to improve MOPT performance in the fields of road design, maintenance and work
supervision, ensuring both full implementation of the recommendations and on-
the-job training of MOPT's personnel.
B. Descrintion of the Technical Assistance Pro-r
2.02     The Technical Assistance Program aims at: i) developing and
implementing a highway maintenance management system; ii) defining new
standards for pavement structural design and rehabilitation; iii) upgrading
procedures for the supervision of road works; iv) assisting in the
implementation of the computerized management information system developed
under Loan 2829-CO; v) preparing a plan of action to facilitate intermodal
transport. A brief description of the scope of each technical assistance is
presented below, while outlines of the terms of reference for the first three
tasks are shown in Attachments 1 to 3.
2.03     Highway Maintenance Management.  Consultants will develop a
comprehensive highway maintenance management system (including equipment
management) and would assist MOPT in its implementation in both MOPT
headquarters and districts. The technical assistance will focus on: i)
reviewing current maintenance procedures, policies and organizational
capabilities within MOPT's headquarters and districts; ii) upgrading the Road
Maintenance Inventory, organized on the basis of homogeneous road segments;
iii) defining performance criteria and standards for maintenance activities,
adjusted to regional variations; iv) implementing a comprehensive maintenance
management system to effectively plan, schedule, execute and/or control
maintenance works carried out by force account, microempresas and large
contractors, assisted by automatization at both central and district levels;
v) implementing a comprehensive equipment management system, directed at
establishing equipment operating costs, evaluating equipment maintenance
policies and procedures, and managing inventories of spare parts; vi) setting
up cost accounting procedures to support the maintenance and equipment
management systems; vii) implementing reporting procedures, designed to
provide timely, useful and accurate information; viii) establishing an
effective maintenance policy, tailored to the local existing conditions and
constraints; ix) formulating a three-year road maintenance program and



- 130 -
ANNEX 7
Page 4 of 8
performance budget, coordinated with other highway investments (road
construction and rehabilitation); and x) training MOPT's personnel. The
consultants would establish and make operational the system for road
maintenance by December, 1993.
2.04     Standards for Structural Pavement Desiqn and Road Rehabilitation.  A
short term consultant will guide and review MOPT's task force work in order to
produce a Pavement Catalogue or a comprehensive but practical Pavement Design
Guide, encompassing both new pavement design and rehabilitation of existing
pavements. The consultant's selection will be based on his/her background in
pavement design, evaluation and research, and participation in similar
studies. Consultant services will include: i) establishing a general
framework and methodology for task force work; ii) defining inputs and models
required to characterize and analyze pavements performance in existing local
conditions (traffic, environment, available materials and technologies); iii)
reviewing and upgrading existing procedures to evaluate soils and road
materials in engineering studies; iv) assessing research needs to improve or
validate the design procedures or to promote new technologies; v) upgrading
the terms of reference for pavement design studies; vi) reviewing the final
reports and supporting documents prepared by the task force; and vii)
assisting MOPT in the implementation of the procedures and standards developed
by the technical assistance. A pavement design guide would be completed by
June, 1993.
2.05     Supervision of road works.  Technical assistance services will
provide for the preparation and implementation of upgraded supervision
procedures, work programming and monitoring techniques and cost estimates
manuals. It will be carried out by a specialist in highway construction and
supervision, over a period of 12 months to foster not only the development of
such manuals and procedures but their immediate use in on-going construction
works. The consultant services will include: i) reviewing existing supervision
procedures; ii) assessing and reporting main deficiencies in construction
monitoring and technical supervision through frequent visits to projects under
construction; iii) developing guidelines to improve construction supervision
and project monitoring; iv) developing a comprehensive construction cost
estimate manual, suitable to account for regional (climate, labor market)
variations; v) assisting MOPT's task force in the preparation of the new
technical specifications; vi) assessing training needs among private
supervisors and contractors to ensure a smooth transition to new procedures
and specifications; and vii) providing on-the-job training to MOPT's staff. A
civil works supervision manual would be issued by June, 1993.
2.06     Imolementation of the Computerized Manacement Information System.
The system is being developed by a local consulting firm and will be in place
by mid 1992. Many managerial units will be involved in MOPT and sector
parastatals. Short term intermittent local consultant services would
facilitate and speed up a full use of the systems in specific fields where
their need may arise. Among others, consultant services will include: i)
training MOPT staff in the use of the different sub-systems; ii) solving and
trouble-shooting operating problems; iii) improving the procedures for data



- 131 -
ANNEX 7
Page 5 of 8
collection and data validation; and iv) monitoring a consistent system
implementation through out MOPT and sector parastatals units.
2.07     Intermodal Transport Facilitation.  A full-time international trade
specialist will assist MOPT in preparing a plan of action to facilitate
international transport procedures and regulations, and coordinating all
training activities under the project related to intermodal transport
facilitation. This technical assistance will cover a period of two years.
C. Description of the Studies
2.08     The scope of the four studies included in this component covers: i)
decentralization of the management, construction and maintenance of the
national road network; ii) behavior and performance of road materials and
pavements; iii) road construction industry; and iv) river maintenance. A
brief description of the scope of each study is presented below, while
outlines of the terms of reference for all studies are shown in Attachments 4
to 7.
2.09    Decentralization of the Management. Construction and Maintenance of
the National Road Network. As part of the Government's decentralization
policies, MOPT plans to transfer to the Departments the responsibilities for
road construction and maintenance over a portion of the national road network.
The proposed study aims at defining the optimal size of the national road
network and the appropriate transference mechanisms -including human, physical
and financial resources- to ensure a gradual and smooth implementation during
a transition period of 3 to 5 year, tailored to the absorptive capacity of
each departmental agency. The study will comprise: i) defining the optimal
extent of the national, regional and departmental road networks; ii) designing
a gradual strategy to effectively reassign the functions of road construction
and maintenance at the national, regional and departmental level; iii)
evaluating the absorptive capacity and resource availability among the
Departmeits; iv) establishing the criteria and mechanisms to effectively
transfer human resources, equipment fleets and maintenance facilities from
MOPT to the departmental agencies; v) establishing the criteria and mechanisms
to effectively transfer existing financial resources from the national level
to the departmental level, generate additional financial sources and obtain
external aid at the departmental and regional levels; vi) defining standards
for project planning, budgeting and monitoring results in the public works
sector within the Departments; and vii) proposing an action plan to organize
the transference of responsibilities and resources to the Departments.
2.10     Behavior and Performance of Road Materials and Pavements.  The
proposed study will follow-up the pavement research started in 1984 through an
agreement with the University of Cauca. The three-year program would cover:
i) evaluating road materials behavior using modern laboratory testing
techniques; ii) studying the impact of environmental factors in pavement
design and performance; iii) monitoring pavement performance through periodic
structural and functional evaluation of the existing pavement in selected road



- 132 -
ANNEX 7
Page 6 of 8
sectiona spread around the road network; iv) establishing tentative pavement
deterioration curves for different designs and environments; and v) selecting
experimental sections for trial of non-conventional cost-effective solutions
and validation of the research findings.
2.11     Road Construction Industry.  The proposed study would address
existing constraints in the private road construction industry to enable a
more efficient participation in MOPT's road and maintenance work programs and
delineate an action plan to overcome those constraints. Cyclical variations
of Government's road construction investments in the past, a chronic shortage
of funds to finance on-going civil works, cumbersome procurement and contract
administration procedures, and serious financial restrictions have been
hampering a healthy growth of the industry. The study will analyze these
areas as well as other major aspects such as wages, employment, taxation, use
of technology and training.
2.12     River Maintenance.  Ade.quate maintenance of the Magdalena River is
absolutely essential if the navigation is to be reliable. However,
maintenance has been inadequate in recent years because of poor availability
of the old fleet of dredgers and inefficiencies in operation of the fleet.
The proposed study aims at restructuring the maintenance activities of the
DGNP, looking into the possibility of chartering out MOPT's dredgers to
private operators and submitting a definitive plan for implementing a
satisfactory maintenance program of the Magdalena River.
D. Studies to be carried out through MOPT Task Forces.
2.13     MOPT would carry out through task forces the review and updating of
several important documents related to the preparation of road engineering
studies: i) updated technical specifications for road construction; ii) new
standards for road geometric design; and iii) guidelines for the preparation
of road final engineering studies.  The task force approach would also ensure
full follow through within MOPT on the final recommendations. Consultants
hired under the Technical Assistance Program, study tours abroad financed by
both the proposed loan and Loan 2829-CO, and additional training provided
under the Training Program would assist MOPT task forces in the preparation of
such documents. Orientation seminars will be held in 1992-1993 to teach the
use of these documents to contractors and consultants (see Annex 8).
2.14     Technical Soecifications.  The technical specifications currently in
use were prepared in 1970. The last attempt to upgrade them was carried out
in 1988, using a consulting firm, but the new version was rejected by MOPT and
road-related local private agencies!/. Staffed by five key division chiefs
and other MOPT professionals, the task force would review the 21 most relevant
specifications by june 1992, according to the agreed work schedule, and
1/AICO, Asociacion de Inaenieros Consultores Colombianos, and ACIC, Asociacion
Colombiana de Inaenieros Constructores.



- 133 -
Page 7 of 8
complete the final document by December, 1992. Task force's work schedule
comprises seven phases: i) reviewing of current technical specifications
content and suitability; ii) upgrading task force skills through a study tour
abroad; iii) preparing upgraded technical specifications; iv) reviewing the
preliminary version by MOPT's Council of Works; v) reviewing the preliminary
version by AICO and ACICI vi) issuing the final version; and vii) publishing
and teaching the application of the technical specifications among consultants
and contractors. The work started early in 1991 and the first phase has
already been completed.
2.15     Standards for road ceometric design.  The existing standardsv are
extremely demanding, particularly when defining curve ratios, maximum slopes,
roadway and shoulder widths in mountainous terrain. Consequently, MOPT very
often has to introduce changes in on-going project standards in order to
reduce construction costs. For the same reason, consultants frequently use
their own design standards resulting in inconsistent variations through out
the projects life. The study carried out by consultants in 1988 did not
produce a set of practical standards, but a compilation of local and foreign
criteria. MOPT task force will review these documents and upgrade geometric
standards to be compatible with local conditions by December 1992, according
to the following work schedule: i) upgrading task force skills through
training abroad (to be carried out under Loan 2829-CO); ii) reviewing
available documents and determining geometric design criteria and standards;
iii) discussing proposed standards with AICO and the University of Cauca; iv)
preparing the final document; and v) publishing and teaching the use of the
standards for road geometric design among consultants.
2.16     Guidelines for the oregaration of final engineering studies. MOPT is
working on upgrading terms of reference for the preparation of final
engineering studies, which would prevent variations in the quality and scope
of these studies. The new standards for road geometric design, structural
pavement design and road rehabilitation, as well as the updated technical
specifications, which will be developed under the proposed Project, will form
part of the guidelines. A preliminary version of the document would be
finished by June 1992; the final version would be issued once the new
procedure for pavement design is fully developed, by mid 1993.
3. Cost of the Technical Assistance Component.
3.01     The total cost of the technical assistance component financed by the
proposed Project is estimated at US$2.36 million. The project will finance
100% of expenditures in both foreign and local currency. Detailed costs for
each technical assistance and study are shown in the following table.
