3. Organization and Implementation of the Traininc Proaram.
A. Institutional Implementation Mechanisms.
3.01     Two organizational units will provide the permanent structure
required to develop a sound and permanent training policy, implement the
resulting training programs and evaluate the progress in achieving their
objectives: i) the Department of Industrial Relations; and ii) the Training
Advisory Committee.
a) The Department of Industrial Relations (DIR). The organization of
MOPT's Training Unit is shown in Figure 1. Two sections under the
Division of Personnel are specifically related to training: i) Selection
of Personnel and Training (SPT), which develops and monitors training
progrems; and ii) Register and Control (SRC), which keeps record of
staff achievements and training received. Staffed by a section chief
and five professionals, SPT depends mainly on external sources to
develop and deliver training programs; although it administers a
training center for mechanics, the eLght MOPT instructors who carry out
the program are not functionally and budgetaryly dependent on DIR but on
some districts. Four measures were agreed to upgrade DIR's training
abilities and performance: i) strengthening its skills to plan,
organize, coordinate, implement, and evaluate training programs, through
technical assistance and internships abroad, as stated in para. 2.02;
ii) providing adequate resources and repositloning MOPT instructors
under DIR's budget; iii) linking its work with operational units through
an established Training Advisory Committee; and iv) implementing
procedures for enforcing accountability and conferring responsibility
for training, through a policy sustained by MOPT authorities.
b) The Trainina Advisory committee. Staffed by the heads of key
departments of the Directorate of Roads and led by the Director of
Industrial Relations, this committee has been working on an informal
basis during project preparation and it should be appointed in order to
continue during project implementation. Its main task is to advise the
Director of Industrial Relations in matters such as training strategies,
priorities, and organization. The committee will provide guidance for:
i) establishing training needs and training priorities in each
organizational unit; ii) developing new programs and evaluating the
effectiveness of current programs; and iii) identifying external
conferences and seminars tha; may be useful for the staff in each unit.
3.02     While the Technical Advisory Committee will be primarily concerned
with the processes relating to the identification of training needs and the
evaluation of results, DIR will have the main responsibility for designing and
delivering the training program and the overall supervisory and coordinating
role in making sure that the training program is fully implemented as approved
by MOPT authorities.

3.03     The most successful training area in the past (productive courses for
mechanics) was based on the principle that training accreditation was
necessary for performing Lhe assigned job and a requirement for promotion.
Making more visible the role of SRC, and implementing a job description manual
to guide MOPT staff promotion would contribute largely to this purpose. A job
description manual, defining skills and knowledge accreditation required by
all MOPT categories and occupations, will be completed through SENA and
consultant assistance during project implementation. Additionally,
participation in MOPT training groups and task forces would also be accredited
and taken into account when selectirg candidates for study tours and
conferences abroad.
B. Preparation of the Training Procram.
3.04     During project preparation, DIR made preliminary estimates of MOPT
current and projected training needs with the collaboration of key division
managers. Target groups were identified and sized through a preliminary
occupational analysis. Based on this information, a tentative program was
delineated; a series of courses was proposed defining for each Dne its main
objective, target group, method of training, required resources, tentative
organization (instructors, course duration, participants and location) and the
projected number of times it would be repeated during the four year program
period to ensure it reaches a high percentage of the target group. Although
this program is still tentative and adjustments would be needed in order to
reflect changes in M04?T needs and priorities, the procedure developed for its
preparation is valuable and sets the basis for its periodical updating.



- 181 -
Page 8 of 10
C. Trainina Facilities and Equipment.
3.05     In order to provide appropriate training facilities, MOPT would
recondition three classrooms in its headquarters and relocate in the eigth
district's facilities the existing training center, an old 1800m2 building
located close to Bogota's downtown. Although provisions for the
rehabilitation of this facility were included in Loan 2668-CO, the idea was
finally discarded due to its high cost and the lack of functionality of its
location. The relocated facility, much smaller, would be used mainly for
mechanics training while the classrooms for delivering courses and seminars
for MOPT's headquarter staff.
3.06     Additional training equipment and workehop tools, computer supplies,
furniture and training aids would be provided to facilitate and improve the
quality and efficiency of the training process. In order to enhance the basic
infrastructure for training at district level, two transportable workshops and
classrooms would be purchased. On the basis of agreed schedules, the
districts would assign in-service machines and areas for practice for heavy
machine operators training, and worn equipment for productive courses for
training of mechanics.
D. Execution of the Training Program.
3.07     The execution of the training program includes: i) delivery of 735
courses in Colombia to provide training for about 6,900 MOPT's officials and
1000 consultants, microempresas, large contractors and transport operators
personnel; ii) participation of 94 high level staff in conferences, courses
and seminars abroad, including 31 staff-months of internships abroad; iii)
technical assistance for MOPT's training unit through 12 specialist-months of
a local training consultant and eight specialist-months of foreign training
advisors; iv) participation of 16 MOPT's instructors and five training
specialists in internships abroad ot one-month duration.
3.08     Training in heavy equipment operation and maintenance would require
16 full-time instructors recruited from MOPT. In addition, experienced MOPT
personnel would perform as part time instructors in specific technical
subjects. Both full-time and part-time instructors would be trained by DIR cr
SENA in pedagogical subjects and training methodologies. DIR would make use
of occasional (ad-hoc) working groups to assist in the development and
delivery of seminars and courses wherever necessary to ensure training
quality; these groups will be staffed by MOPT specialists in the specific
field and appointed by the Training Advisory Committee. Training of road
foremen, heavy equipment operators and drivers would be carried out in the
districts and mostly in the field, under typical working conditions, while
performing needed maintenance works on specific sections of the roads.
Training of mechanics would be carried out in the district workshops thizough
practical courses ("productive courses") using and repairing worn and
unserviceable equipment. The same approach would be used for developing road
laboratory skills. In other areas, training of MOPT staff, consultants,



- 182 -
ANNEX-8"
Page 9 of 10
contractors and tranport operators would be achieved mainly through short
courses and technical seminars, including exercisos and case studies whenever
applicable; their delivery would require the participation of universities and
training agencies, local consultants and expatriate experts engaged either
directly by MOPT or through agreements with specialized institutions.
E. Administration of the Training Program.
;.9      MOPT's Department of Industrial Relations would be responsible for:
i) planning, organizing, implementing, and monitoring the training program;
ii) establishing agreements with local and foreign training institutions; iii)
administering the internship program for courses abroad and the '-chnical
assistance in training; and iv) evaluating and reporting the rei lts of the
training programs. Within this context, DIR would prepare a training
handbook, describing the training activities scheduled for the incoming year,
which would be distributed to MOPT headquarters and district units. This
handbook would list details of courses and training events, including: course
objective and content; prerequisites or conditions for selecting participants
(wherever applicable); training methods and location; target group (primary
beneficiaries) and career development benefits (wherever applicable); training
organization co-sponsoring the course (wherever applicable); course duration
and scheduled dates. By this means, managers would know in advance key
information pertaining to the overall training program, which would facilitate
sielecting the appropriate training programs for their staff and scheduling
these training activities throughout the yea_ DIR would also organize twice
a year meetings with the districts to discuss the training plan and
implementation problems that could arise from training activities carried out
in the districts. DIR would report annually on training activities carried
out during the year, evaluating the program results, and would prepare and
furnish to MOPT authorities and the Bank an updated and comprehensive plan for
the subsequent year.
4. Cost of the Training Component.
4.01     The total cost of the training component financed by the proposed
Project is estimated at US$2.5 million. The percentage of expenditurets for
each sub-component to be financed is 100% of foreign currency expenditures and
65% of local currency expenditures (90% in the case of local acquisition of
training aids). The amount to be financed by the Bank is estimated at US$2.1
million. The cost breakdown is shown in the following table.

COLOMBIA
THIRD NATIONAL ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
Description of the Training Program
1.       The following tables show the content and scope of MOPT's training
program and the components to be financed under the Project. Courses and
seminars are grouped into ten subject areas: i) road maintenance; ii) heavy
equipment operation; iii) equipment maintenance; iv) road planning and
financ_ng; v) road design, construction and supervision; vi) bridge design and
construction; vii) project environmental evaluation and control; viii)
navigation and ports; ix) intermodal transport facilitation; and x) computer
technology, administration and clerical skills. Training events in Colombia
and abroad are presented in separate tables. The cost breakdown per subject
area is shown below.
Total cost (US$'000)
Subject area                             Local    Abroad     Total
01.  Road maintenance                                (*)     110.0      110.0
02.  Heavy equipment operation                       (*)       -         (*)
03.  Equipment maintenance                           (*)       -         (*)
04.  Road Planning and Financing                    18.4      82.0      100.4
05.  Road design, construction & supervision       314.0     270.0      504.0
06.  Bridge design and construction                120.0      32.0      152.0
07.  Project environmental evaluation & control    120.0     120.0     240.0
08.  Navigation and ports                          140.0     176.0     316.0
09.  Intermodal transport facilitation             200.0       -       200.0
10.  Computer tech., administration & clerical      87.6       -        87.6
____________--______________________-______________________________________
T O T A L                          1,000.0     790.0   1,790.0
__- _______-- ________________________________________________- _____________
(*) The cost of these training events comprise expenditures covered by MOPT's operational budget;
therefore, they are not included in the Project.

COLOMBIA
HIRI NATI      ROADS SECTOR PROJECT
_ut.Lnp_of Terms of Beference for Technical Assistance for MOPT
in Traininofg uOPT's Trainina Unit Personnel
Background
1.       The Directorate of Industrial Relations (DIR) is in charge of the
preparation and implementation of MOPT's training programs. DIR has outlined
a four-year training program to be carried out under the Project. The
organization and delivery of this program involves many sources, including
external consultants hired under technical assistance programs, local experts
and MOPT instructors. A key element for ti.e success of this training program
is the improvement of HOPT's in-house capability to plan, coordinate and
provide efficient training activities.
9bJect:Les
2.       The Technical Assistance aims at strengthening MOPT's training unit
capabilLties to plan, organize, coordinate, implement and evaluate relevant
training programs.
Seoge of the Assistance
3.       The main tasks to be carried out under this technical assistance will
include:
a) establishing and coordinating MOPT's training policy and strategy;
b) updating the manpower training needs and the priority levels for
training, based on occupational studies which will define the skills and
knowledge required by all job categories;
c) defining course proposals, objectives and evaluation criteria;
d) establishing training facilities, learning materials and other resources
required for training delivery;
e) preparing guidelines for course development and a training handbook,
describing the courses and training events available throughout the year
and providing basic information on course content, objectives, target
group and carrer development benefita, prerequisites for selecting
participants, training methods, location, diration and scheduled dates,
etc.;
f) updating and expanding the agreements with external training sources,
such as local or foreign specialized training agencies, universities or
specialized equipment supplier firms;  '"



- 191 -
ANNEX 8
Attachment 2
Page 2 of 2
g) designing a monitoring and evaluation system for following up the
overall implementation of the training program ;
h) evaluating and reporting the program achievements during the foregoing
year and updating the schedules for the incoming year; and
i) upgrading the skills of the personnel in charge of administering and
implementing the training programs.
Staffing
4.       The technical assistance will be carried out by a consultant over a
period of 12 months. The consultant will be selected on the basis of his/her
background in managing training activities, particularly in establishing
facilities and training programs for personnel involved in road
administration. The consultant will take part in active training by conducting
seminars for MOPT managers and discussions with trainers assigned to
delivering the training program to ensure they achieve an appropriate level of
pedagogical skills.
Executina Aoenuv and Counterpart Staff
5.       The Directcrate of Industrial Relations (DIR) will be the technical
assistance executing agency. MOPT will choose from DIR's permanent staff the
counterpart personnel to be assigned to the technical assistance.
Schedule and ReDorts
6.       The technical assistance will start early in 1992. The consultant
will submit the following reports:
a) an initial report presented by the end of the second month, reviewing
MOPT's training program, policies and priorities, and proposing an
action plan to implemei.t upgraded practices during the technical
assistance;
b) a training handbook issued by the end of the sixth month, describing the
training courses and events available through MOPT's training program;
c) a final report, evaluating the achievements of the training program
carried out and summarizing the tasks performed by the technical
assistance as well as recommendations for future implementation. The
consultant will furnish to MOPT a preliminary version of this report 30
days before the end of the contract and will issue the revised version,
upon MOPT's approval, by the contract's final date.

COLOMBIA
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT OF THE MIDDLE MAGDALENA RIVER
NAVIGATION WORKS SUB-PROJECT OF THE THIRD NATIONAL HIGHWAYS PROJECT
1.   Prior to World Bank approval of financing for this Sub-project, MOPT
needs to complete an Environmental Impact Study (EIS). It is expected that
MOPT will hire an interdisciplinary team of consultants, with expertise that
includes aquatic ecolcgy and fish biology, hydrology, environmental
engineering, and sociology or anthropology. This consulting team should be
independent of the principal engineering and design contractor for this Sub-
project (to avoid possible conflicts of interest in evaluating alternative
Sub-project designs). The consulting team should work closely with
counterparts from MOPT's regular staff, to provide them with in-service
environmental training.
2.   To satisfy the Bank's requirements, the EIS report should contain all of
the features listed below. The EIS report's recommendations should also be
consistent with the Bank's relevant environmental policies, particularly the
Operational Directive on Environmental Assessment and the Wildlands Policy
(colnies attached).  Additional technical guidance is provided in the Bank's
draft Environmental Assessment Sourcebook (relevant chaptero are being sent to
MOPT under separate cover).
3.   The EA report (written in Spanish) should, at a minimum, contain:
(a) An Executive Summary (in English as well as Spanish);
(b) A discussion of the leaal. policy, and administrative framework
within which the EA is prepared. This would include a brief
discussion of all relevant Colombian environment-related laws and
regulations, as well as the World Bank's environmental policies;               k
(c)  A detailed description of all proposed Sub-troiect components,
including the precise geographical locations and timing of
construction for all river works and other infrastructure. The
broader "area of influence" of the Project's possible environmental
impacts should be defined and indicated on a map. Any
"complementary" infrastructure not financed under this Sub-project
(but relevant to possible cumulative environmental impacts) should
also be similarly described. This would include (at a minimum) the
river navigation works proposed (for IDB financing) between Puerto
Berrio and Barrancabermeja, as well as the construction of any
planned new river access roads that would be made economically
viable as a result of this Sub-project;
(d) Baseline environmental data on the relevant physical, biological,
and socio-economic features of the project's area of influence.
These include:



- 193 -
ANNEX 9
Page 2 of 6
(i)       River hydrology, and aeomorDholoav, particularly the
dynamics of natural changes in the river's channels.
Also, seasonal data on rainfall, flooding patterns, and
groundwater;
(ii)      The different types of aouatic and terrestrial
ecosystems, including those found in the Magdalena
River's major and minor channels, cienegas and other
wetlands, river islands, beaches and shorelines, and
other lands within the Sub-project's area of influence.
The report should also indicate whether any of these
areas occur within existing or proposed conservation
units (managed by INDERENA or any other entity);
(iii)     A relatively complete list of the fish. other acuatic and
terrestrial vertebrates, and native plant species which
regularly occur in the Sub-project's area of influence.
Particularly important is a list of all the fish and
other aquatic vertebr tes which are endemic to the
Magdalena River (i.e., found nowhere else on Earth) and
are known or suspected to occur within the Sub-project's
area of influence. Other fish, animal, and plant species
of local economic importance, or particularly likely to
be affected by Sub-project activities, should also be
highlighted;
(iv)      Information on local human communities, including
population size and projected growth, principal economic
production systems (such as different types of
agriculture, fishing, trading and services, and oil
industry labor), e.nployment, land tenure, income
distribution, access to public services, public health
problems, and any major social tensions or conflicts;
(v)       The presence of any cultural Droperties, including
archaeological, historic, and sacred religious sites;
(vi)      Existing and projected (even without the Project) future
human uses of the Magdalena River and adjacent lands, how
they compare with the existing natural resource base
(including land use capability and water availability),
and the extent to which they are legal and in accordance
with official Government plans for the area; and
(vii)     Ongoing and proposed development activities with
significant environmental effects (but not directly
related to the proposed Project);
(e) All significant environmental imnacts (direct and indirect) likely
to result from the proposed Sub-project should be identified and
(to the maximum extent possible) quantified. The EIS report should



- 194 -
ANNEX 95
Page 3 of 6
indicate specifically how the environmental features described in
Para. 3.d. would change as a result of the Sub-project. The
pousible cumulative environmental effects from complementary
infrastructure investments (as noted in Para. 3.c.) should also be
described. Anticipated environmental impacts should be discussed
in terms of their probability of occurence, physical magnitude,
duration (whether temporary or permanent), reversibility, and
compensability. Particular attention should be given to discussing
the following possible environmental impacts:
(i)       Eftects on fish and other aauatic life.  What are the
expected (absolute and relative) changes in the
populations of each of the endemic, threatened, or
commercially valuable aquatic vertebrate species
occurring in the Sub-project's area of influence? Would
the survival of any endemic species be seriously
threatened as a result of the Sub-project? rf
significant reductions in commercially valuable species
appear likely, the economic and local employment
implications should be quantitatively estimated. The EIS
should assess in detail the possible effects on aquatic
life of Sub-project-induced factors such as:
(1) The dry-season reduction in the physical area of
available river habitat (due to the dessication of
many river channels);
(2)  Ecological changes in the main (unblocked) river
channels, due to dredging and increased dry-season
flow; and
(3) The possibility that the closed-off channels would
function as seasonal "deathtraps" for fish and other
aquatic life, by permitting water and aquatic life
to enter and exit during the wet season, but not the
dry season;
(ii)      Hvdrolocv-related environmental impacts, including
possible changes in (1) water flows to cienagas and other
wetlands, (2) the river's sediment load, and (3) river
bank and channel erosion patterns (including those
downriver of the project site);
(iii)     The environmental effects of disposina dredced material,
such as reducing the habitat of native animals and
plants, or covering up possible cultural property sites;
(iv)      The environmental implications of seasonally connecting
some river islands to the mainland (by drying out certain
river channels), in terms of human settlement or land use
changes, access by predators to bird or turtle nesting



- 195 -
ANNEX9
Page 4 of 6
areas, and other possible effects;
(v)       The possible increase Li accidental oil spills and other
water pollution, resulting from increased barge traffic;
and
(vi)      The ecological and socio-economic implications of induced
develooment along the river's edge and along river access
roads, in response to increased ship traffic.
(f)  Analysis of alternatives in Sub-project design (including siting
and technologies used) and operation, to minimize negative
environmental impacts. All types of alternatives which could
achieve the project's economic objectives (e.g., reduced
transportation costs for particular goods) should be considered.
Each alternative should be evaluated in terms of its environmental
impact, investment and recurrent costs, and institutional,
training, and monitoring requirements. The least environmentally
damaging alternative must be selected and incorporated within
project design, unless there are compelling reasons not to do so
(for example, if the financial costs were to be prohibitive). Any
departure from recommending the least environmentally damaging
alternative mus_ be explicitly justified within the EIS. In any
case, the recommended alternative must be fully compatible with the
Bank's environmental policies;
(g)  A Proiect-specific Environmental Management Plan, which would
outline in detail all measures needed to minimize, mitigate, or
cozr*nsate for any expected environmental damage from the Project.
This Plan should include:
(i)       A precise division of institutional responsibilities for
its implementation (including monitoring of compliance
and of environmental quality) between MOPT and other
(national and local) Government agencies, including
recommendations for any legal convenios which may be
needed;
*(ii)      A timetable of planned activities (which must be properly
synchronized with Project construction plans);
(iii)     A budget (which should include a discussion of how the
Plan would be fully financed); and
(iv)      An estimate of the time (in staff weeks per year) and
technical qualifications of environmental staff required
within MOPT and other agencies for effective Plan
implementation;
(h) Summaries of comments provided by Government and non-Governmental
organizations on the draft EA and the proposed Sub-project itself



- 196 -
ANNEX 9
Page 5 of 6
(see below), along with a brief discussion of how these comments
were incorporated within the final EA. The final EA should also
mention those comments which were rejected as inadvisable, and
specifically indicate why those comments were rejected; and
(i) Important su0olementarv information, including a bibliography of
references (published and unpublished), maps, aerial or ground
photographs, technical diagrams, etc.
4.   Within two months of beginning work on the EIS, MOPT should organize
approximately two formal meetings to obtain constructive suggestions for
carrying out the EIS and for improving the design and implementation of the
Project's environmental components. One of these meetings should be with all
interested national and local Government agencies, including those in charge
of environmental regulation, protected areas, land and water management,
fisheries and wildlife, and national and local development planning. The
other meeting should be with interested non-Governmental parties, which should
include environmental NGOs; local community groups; independent experts (such
as consultants and university professors); and any other interested members of
the public.  The written invitations for both meetings should include a copy
of the terms of reference (and draft table of contents, if available) for the
EIS, a brief description of the proposed Project, a small-scale map of the
main Project areas, and a chronogram of the planned EIS preparation.
5.   After a draft EIS is completed, it must be formally reviewed by INDERENA
(in accordance with Colombian law) before any Sub-project construction can
begin. Copies of the draft EA should also be sent to the same Governmental
and non-Governmental representatives who participated in the earlier two
meetings (discussed in the above paragraph); these persons should be invited
to provide comments on the draft EIS. These comments can be in writing, or
presented orally at one of about two additional formal meetings (one for
Governmental and one for non-Governmental representatives), which NOPT should
specifically convene to review the findings and recommendations of the draft
EIS.  MOPT should take all constructive comments into account in finalizing
the draft EIS.
6.   During the process of preparing the EIS, MOPT should send brief (about 5
pages) bi-monthly progress reports to the World Bank. These reports should
indicate:
(a) Which major tasks have been accomplished, and which remain to be
done;
(b) The pace of progress in completing the EA, and whether any delays
are expected;
(c) Whether or not it appears likely that the Sub-project can be
designed to comply in full with Colombian environmental laws and
with the World Bank's environmental policies.  (These are necessary
conditions for Bank financing of this Sub-project.);



- 197 -
ANNEX 9
Page 6 of 6
(d) Any surprising findings which could substantially change Project
design or implementation plans; and
(e) Any unforseen problems which the Bank could help resolve.
i
7.   The EA report should be no longer than is necessary to convey all of the
information needed for proper environmental evaluation and design of the
Project. Most of this information is outlined above. Making the EA report
exceptionally long will not make it more useful.
8.   The final EA should be made available to interested members of the
public (free or at a nominal charge, if needed to cover reproduction costs).
